Razi's Classification and Critique of Definition
Razi classifies definition into four types and critiques each, followed by commentator notes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's division of definition into four types and his arguments that each is logically impossible.

Core Idea
Razi argues that no definition of a quiddity is possible without circularity or infinite regress.

Razi, in his Muhassal, presents a foundational critique of definition. He first classifies all possible ways to define an essence, then systematically shows that each leads to contradiction. This lesson walks through that classification and the key arguments, along with a commentator's nuanced responses.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification and initial arguments
Start by reading the main passage where Razi lists four types of definition. Notice the immediate rejection of self-definition and definition by internal parts.
The text is dense; take it slowly.
2
Focus on the critique of self-definition and internal parts
Understand why defining by itself is circular, and why defining by its parts either repeats the whole or demands prior definitions.
Pay attention to the distinction between aggregate and parts.
3
Examine the critique of external attributes
Razi argues that an external attribute cannot uniquely identify a quiddity because different quiddities share attributes, leading to infinite regress.
This is the most nuanced argument.
4
Review the commentator's responses
The commentator offers four defenses, challenging Razi's claims about the aggregate, the need to define parts, the concept of definition by external attributes, and the effect of the whole.
These responses are not refutations but refinements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أن تعريف الماهية إما أن يكون بنفسها أو بما يكون داخلا فيها أو بما يكون خارجا عنها أو بما يتركب من الأخيرين ، أما تعريفها بنفسها فحال ؛ لأن المعرف معلوم قبل المعرف ، فلو عرفنا الشيء بنفسه لزم تقدم العلم به تعلى العلم به وهو محال ، وأما تعريفها بالأمور الداخلة فيها في حالى ؛ لأن تعريفها إما أن يكون بمجموع تلك الأمور وهو باطل؛ لأنه نفس ذلك المجموع ، فتعريفها بذلك المجموع تعريف النيء بنفسه وهو محال (٢) أو ببعض أجزائها وهو محال، لأن تعريف الماهية المركبة لا يمكن إلا بواسطة تعريف أجزائها (٣) . فلو كان جزء من فا لها لكان ذلك الجزء معرفا لجميع أجزائها ، فيكون ذلك الجزء معرفا لنفسه رهو محال (٤) والسائر الأجراء وذلك يقتضى كون الشىء معرفا لما يكون خارجا عنه وهو القسم الثالث وهو محال ، لأن الماهيات المختلفة يجوز اشتراكها فى لازم واحد ، وإذا كان كذلك فالوصف الخارجي لا يفيد تعريف الماهية الموصوف إلا إذا عرف أن ذلك الموصوف هذا الموصوف به دون كل ما عداه، لكن العلم بهذا يتوقف على تصور ذلك الموصوف وعلى تصور كل ما عداه وذلك محال، أما الأول فلانه يلزم منه الدور وأما الثاني فلأنه يقتضى تقدم تصور جميع الماهيات التي لا نهاية لها على سبيل الفصيل (١) وأما تعريفها بما يتركب من الداخل والخارج فبطلان ما تقدم من الأفسام يقتضى بطلانه (٢.
(1) أقول : قوله إن مجموع أجزاء الماعية هو نفوس الماهية ليس بصحيح لأن الجزء متقدم على الكل بالطبع ، والأشياء التى كل واحد منها متقدم على شيء منأخر عنها يمتنع أن تكون نفس المتأخر ، ويجوز أن تصير عند الاجتماع ماهيته هى المتأخرة فيتحصل معرفتها بها ، كما أن العلم بالجنس والفصل وبالركيب التقييدى متقدم على العلم بالجنس المقيد بالفصل وهى أجزاؤه وبهما يحصل العلم به .
(۳) أقول : لو قال تعريف الماهية المركبة لا يمكن إلا بواسطة معرفة أجزائها لكان أصوب ، إذ من الجائز أن تكون الأجزاء غير محتاجة إلى التعريف (٤) أقول : هذه الدعوى غير صحيحة لم يتم عليها حجة ، فإن من الجائز أن تكون الأجزاء كلها أو بعضها معرفة الماهية ولا يلزم منه أن يكون معرفا لجميع أجزاء الماهية حتى لنفسه فإنا بينا أن الماهية مغايرة الأجزاء كلها ، وإنما وقع هذا الغلط من عدم التمييز بين الماهية وبين أجزائها كلها .
(1) أقول : تعريف الموصوف يتوقف على كون الوصف المعرف بحيث ينتقل الذهن من تصوره إلى تصور ماهية الموصوف لا على العلم يكون ذلك الوصف كذلك حتى يلزم المجال الذي ذكره، وأما كون الموصوف هو الموصوف بذلك الوصف دون كل ما عداه ليقن كون الوصف إما مساويا للوصوف وإما أخص منه والأول كالضاحك للإنسان والثاني كالكاتب له وعلى التقديرين يكون الوصف ملزوما والموصوف لازما ، واللزوم إن كان عقليا انتقل المقل من تصور الملزوم إلى تصور الازم فيحصل التعريف ، ولا يكون اللعلم باللزوم شرطا فى الانتقال ، فلا يلزم ذلك المحال . والتعريف فى الأول يكون مطردا منعكسا وفى الثاني مطردا غير منعكس ، والذى ذكره من كون الوصف لازما للماهيات المختلفه على سبيل الاشتراك لا يحصل التعريف به ، وإن جعل معرفا كان للتعريف منعكسا غير مطرد
(2) أقول : هذا الكلام يقتضى وجوب كون كل واحد من أجزاء المعرف معرفا، وامتناع أن يكون للمجموع أثر غير ما يكون لكل واحد من أجزائه بطلانه ظاهر ، فإن قبل المجموع من حيث هو مجموع غير الاجزاء وهو خارج عن الماهية ، أديب بما مر من جواز كون الخارج معرفا .
Second: The definition of the quiddity (mahiyya) is either by itself, or by what is internal to it, or by what is external to it, or by a combination of the latter two. As for defining it by itself, it is impossible, because the definiendum is known before the definiens. If we defined a thing by itself, it would require that knowledge of it precede knowledge of it, which is impossible. As for defining it by matters internal to it, it is also impossible; because its definition is either by the aggregate of those matters, which is false because it is that same aggregate, so defining it by that aggregate is defining a thing by itself, which is impossible. Or by some of its parts, which is impossible, because the definition of a composite quiddity is not possible except through definition of its parts. If a part of it were to define all its parts, then that part would be a definer of itself, which is impossible. And the rest of the parts would require that a thing be a definer of what is outside it, which is the third division, and it is impossible, because different quiddities may share a single concomitant. If that is so, then the external description does not benefit the definition of the described quiddity unless it is known that this described is described by that description to the exclusion of everything else. But knowledge of this depends on conceiving that described and on conceiving everything else, which is impossible. As for the first, it entails a vicious circle; as for the second, it requires the prior conception of all infinite quiddities in detail. As for defining it by a combination of internal and external, the falsity of the previous divisions entails its falsity.
(1) I say: His statement that the aggregate of the parts of the quiddity is the very quiddity is not correct, because the part is naturally prior to the whole, and things each of which is prior to something subsequent cannot be the very subsequent. It is possible that when they come together, its quiddity becomes the subsequent, so knowledge of it is obtained through them, just as knowledge of the genus and differentia and the restrictive composition is prior to knowledge of the genus qualified by the differentia, and they are its parts, and through them knowledge of it is obtained.
(3) I say: If he had said 'the definition of a composite quiddity is not possible except through knowledge of its parts,' it would be more correct, since it is possible that the parts do not need definition.
(4) I say: This claim is not correct; no proof has been established for it. For it is possible that all or some of the parts are definers of the quiddity, and it does not necessitate that it be a definer of all parts of the quiddity, not even of itself. For we have shown that the quiddity is distinct from all its parts. This error occurred only from the failure to distinguish between the quiddity and all its parts.
(1) I say: The definition of the described depends on the defining description being such that the mind moves from its conception to the conception of the described quiddity, not on the knowledge that the description is such, such that the impossibility he mentioned would follow. As for the described being the one described by that description to the exclusion of everything else, that is because the description is either coextensive with the described or more specific. The first is like 'risible' for man, the second like 'writer' for him. In both cases the description is a concomitant (lazim) and the described is a necessary concomitated (malzum). If the concomitance is rational, the intellect moves from the conception of the concomitant to the conception of the necessary, and the definition is obtained. Knowledge of the concomitance is not a condition for the transfer, so that impossibility does not follow. The definition in the first case is uniform and convertible; in the second it is uniform but not convertible. What he mentioned – that the description is a concomitant of different quiddities by way of commonality – does not yield a definition with it, and if it is made a definer, the definition would be convertible but not uniform.
(2) I say: This speech necessitates that each part of the definiens be a definer, and the impossibility of the whole having an effect other than what each of its parts has is obviously false. For if the whole as a whole is other than the parts, and it is external to the quiddity, it is answered by what has passed about the permissibility of something external being a definer.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage presents a classification and critique of the possible ways to define an essence (mahiyya), followed by commentator responses. The author first lists four methods: (1) definition by itself, (2) by its internal parts, (3) by external attributes, or (4) by a combination of internal and external. Each is shown to be impossible: self-definition is circular; definition by internal parts either reduces to self-definition (if using all parts) or requires prior definition of parts (if using some); definition by externals fails because different essences may share the same external attribute, so it cannot uniquely identify the described; the fourth is ruled out by the failure of the others. The commentator then offers several critiques: he challenges the claim that the aggregate of parts is the essence, arguing that parts are prior to the whole; he notes a possible looseness in the statement that parts must be defined; he denies the charge that a part defining others leads to self-definition, since the essence is distinct from its parts; he reinterprets definition by external description as relying on a mental transfer from concomitant to necessary, without requiring prior knowledge of the whole class of other things; finally, he defends the possibility that a composite definition has an effect beyond its parts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that definition by external properties like 'risible' is always valid because it uniquely identifies a species.
Correction: Razi argues that such definition still requires knowledge that no other quiddity shares that property, which is impossible without knowing all quiddities. The commentator adds that the mental transfer from concomitant to necessary can work without this knowledge if the concomitance is rational.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why is definition by external attributes impossible?
A) Because external attributes are accidental and changeable.
B) Because different quiddities may share the same attribute, requiring knowledge of all other quiddities to uniquely identify the described.
C) Because external attributes are not included in the essence.
D) Because definition must be by essential properties only.

Key Takeaway
Razi systematically eliminates all types of definition, exposing the logical difficulties of defining essence, though commentators offer nuanced defenses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that defining a quiddity by external attributes fails because different quiddities may share the same external attribute. When you look at your own life—your achievements, your reputation, your relationships—how often do you rely on external markers to define who you are? Are you ever unsettled by the thought that those same markers could describe someone else entirely? Will you commit to seeking a deeper, more essential understanding of yourself beyond these externals?
?
The commentator says that the mind can move from a concomitant to the necessary without first knowing all other things. In your spiritual life, you use descriptions of God—His mercy, His power, His justice—to know Him. But are you truly convinced that these descriptions uniquely point to Him, or do you entertain doubts that they might apply to other things? How does this affect the sincerity of your worship? Will you examine today whether your concepts of God are truly exclusive to Him?
?
Razi's critique suggests that even self-definition is circular: you must already know something to define it. When you try to define your own identity—your 'self'—have you ever felt trapped in a circle, where any description seems to presuppose the very self you are trying to define? Does that lead you to a sense of humility about how well you truly know yourself? Can you find rest in the fact that your ultimate identity is known by the One who defined you, not by your own attempts?

The Impossibility of Defining All Things and the Soul's Search
Razi presents the impossibility of universal definition and refutes an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why not everything can be defined and how to respond to the objection that the soul seeks conceptions.

Core Idea
Definitions cannot go on forever; they must stop at basic concepts.

Imam Razi begins by arguing that defining everything is impossible due to circularity or infinite regress. He then anticipates an objection: the soul's search for the essence of the angel and spirit seems to require new conceptions. In response, he distinguishes between seeking word explanations and seeking proofs of existence, both of which are forms of assent rather than new simple conceptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the main argument.
2
Identify the two reasons for impossibility
Find the two reasons Razi gives for why not everything can be defined: circularity and infinite regress.
Circularity occurs if the definition itself depends on the defined; infinite regress if you need to conceive all essences first.
3
Analyze the objection and response
Notice the objection about the soul seeking the essence of angel and spirit. Then see how Razi responds by distinguishing two types of seeking.
This shows that not all seeking is for new simple conceptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموصوف وعلى تصور كل ما عداه وذلك محال، أما الأول فلانه يلزم منه الدور وأما الثاني فلأنه يقتضى تقدم تصور جميع الماهيات التي لا نهاية لها على سبيل التفصيل (١) وأما تعريفها بما يتركب من الداخل والخارج فبطلان ما تقدم من الأقسام يقتضى بطلانه (٢). لا يقال : نحن نجد النفس طالبة لتصور ماهية الملك والروح في قولك فيه؟ لانا نقول : ذلك إما طلب تفسير اللفظ أو طلب البرهان على وجود المتصور وكلاهما تصديق (٣). أقول : تعريف الموصوف يتوقف على كون الوصف المعرف بحيث ينتقل الذهن من تصوره إلى تصور ماهية الموصوف لا على العلم يكون ذلك الوصف كذلك حتى يلزم المحال الذي ذكره، وأما كون الموصوف هو الموصوف بذلك الوصف دون كل ما عداه ليقن كون الوصف إما مساويا للموصوف وإما أخص منه والأول كالضاحك للإنسان والثاني كالكاتب له وعلى التقديرين يكون الوصف ملزوما والموصوف لازما ، واللزوم إن كان عقليا انتقل العقل من تصور الملزوم إلى تصور اللازم فيحصل التعريف ، ولا يكون العلم باللزوم شرطا في الانتقال ، فلا يلزم ذلك المحال . والتعريف في الأول يكون مطردا منعكسا وفي الثاني مطردا غير منعكس ، والذي ذكره من كون الوصف لازما للماهيات المختلفة على سبيل الاشتراك لا يحصل التعريف به ، وإن جعل معرفا كان للتعريف منعكسا غير مطرد (٢) أقول : هذا الكلام يقتضى وجوب كون كل واحد من أجزاء المعرف معرفا، وامتناع أن يكون للمجموع أثر غير ما يكون لكل واحد من أجزائه بطلانه ظاهر ، فإن قبل المجموع من حيث هو مجموع غير الأجزاء وهو خارج عن الماهية ، أديب بما مر من جواز كون الخارج معرفا . (٣) أقول : إنا نعرف تفسير لفظ الروح وتعلم يقينا وجوده في كل ذي روح ، ونجد العلماء يتخالفون في ماهيته كما سيذكره هو نفسه ، وليس ما يطلب منه أحد التصديقين اللذين ذكرهما وكذلك كثير من الأشياء تعلم تفسير لفظه ونحس بوجوده ، أو نعلم وجوده قطعا ويكون مع ذلك تصور ماهيته متعذرا على كثير من الناس كالحركة والزمان والمكان وغيرها .
The described (al-mawṣūf) and the conception of everything other than it, and that is impossible. As for the first, because it entails circularity (al-dawr); and as for the second, because it requires the prior conception of all infinite essences in detail (al-tafṣīl). (1) And as for defining it by what is composed of the internal and external, the falsity of the previous divisions necessitates its falsity. (2) It should not be said: we find the soul seeking the conception of the essence (māhiyya) of the angel and the spirit in your statement about it? For we say: that is either a request for explanation of the word (tafsīr al-lafẓ) or a request for proof (burhān) of the existence of the conceived, and both are assent (taṣdīq). (3) I say: The definition of the described depends on the describing term (al-waṣf al-muʿarraf) being such that the mind transfers from its conception to the conception of the essence of the described, not on the knowledge that that describing term is so, such that the impossibility he mentioned follows. As for the described being the one described by that describing term to the exclusion of everything else, because the describing term is either equivalent to the described or more specific. The first, like 'laughing' for man; the second, like 'writer' for him. In both cases, the describing term is a necessary concomitant (lāzim) and the described is a necessary concomitant (lāzim). If the concomitance (luzūm) is rational, the mind transfers from conception of the concomitant (malzūm) to conception of the necessary concomitant (lāzim), and definition occurs. Knowledge of the concomitance is not a condition for the transfer, so that impossibility does not follow. The definition in the first case is coextensive (muṭṭarid munʿakis) and in the second case coextensive but not converse (muṭṭarid ghayr munʿakis). What he mentioned—that the describing term is a necessary concomitant of different essences by way of common participation (ʿalā sabīl al-ishtirāk)—does not yield definition by it, and if it is made a definer, the definition would be converse but not coextensive. (2) I say: This speech necessitates that each part of the definer (al-muʿarraf) must be a definer, and the impossibility that the whole has an effect other than what each of its parts has is obviously false. For if the whole qua whole is other than the parts and is external to the essence (khārij ʿan al-māhiyya), it has been established by what preceded that it is permissible for the external to be a definer. (3) I say: We know the explanation of the word 'spirit' and we know with certainty its existence in every living thing, and we find scholars differing about its essence, as he himself will mention. And what is sought from one is neither of the two assents he mentioned. Likewise for many things: we know the explanation of their word and we sense their existence, or we know their existence with certainty, yet the conception of their essence is impossible for many people, like motion, time, place, and others.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is a proof-and-objection-answer unit concerning the impossibility of defining or conceiving everything. The author first argues that to define a thing by [its description] and by the conception of everything else is impossible for two reasons: it leads to circularity (dawr) or to an infinite regress of prior conceptions. Then he raises a potential objection: we find the soul seeking the conception of the essence of the angel and the spirit, implying that such conception is possible. The answer distinguishes between two types of seeking: either seeking the explanation of the word (tafsīr al-lafẓ) which is a kind of assent (taṣdīq) about meaning, or seeking proof of existence (burhān ʿalā wujūd al-mutaṣawwar) which is also assent. Thus the soul's search does not prove that conception is acquired from scratch; it is rather a request for definition or proof. The author then adds his own notes (أقول): in the first note, he clarifies that definition depends on the mind's transfer from the describing term to the essence, not on prior knowledge of that transfer, so the impossibility does not follow. He also distinguishes between equivalent and more specific describing terms and their logical properties. In the second note, he rejects the claim that each part of a definer must itself be a definer, because the whole qua whole is external to the parts and can be a definer. In the third note, he provides examples like 'spirit' and other concepts whose essence is unknown yet their word and existence are known, showing that the search for conception is not a search for a new simple conception but for explication.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the soul's search for a conception always requires a new simple conception.
Correction: The author clarifies that such search is either a request for word explanation or proof of existence, both of which are forms of assent, not new simple conceptions.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the response to the objection that the soul seeks the conception of the angel and spirit?
A) Such conception is impossible because the soul cannot conceive anything.
B) The search is either a request for explanation of the word or a request for proof of existence, both of which are assent.
C) The soul already has an innate conception of these things.
D) The objection is invalid because the soul never seeks such conceptions.

Key Takeaway
Definitions cannot continue indefinitely; they must stop at foundational concepts, and the soul's search for understanding is either for word meanings or proof of existence, not for new simple conceptions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself struggling to grasp a truth about Allah or the unseen, do you honestly examine whether your desire is for a clear definition of terms or for a convincing proof of existence? How will you make that distinction today?
?
The passage reveals that your soul's longing for understanding might actually be a request for tafsir al-lafz or burhan. Think of a specific belief you struggle with. Is your frustration rooted in expecting a simple conception rather than seeking the right type of knowledge? What concrete step will you take to change that?

Clarifications on the Process of Definition
Razi elaborates on definition mechanisms and provides illustrative examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's clarifications on the conditions of definition, the role of the whole vs. parts, and examples of concepts whose essence is unknown.

Core Idea
Definition works by mental transfer, not by prior knowledge, and the whole can define even if parts cannot.

Imam Razi provides three important clarifications to his earlier argument. First, he explains that definition depends on the mind transferring from the describing term to the essence, not on knowing that the term is a definer. Second, he argues that the whole of a definition can have an effect beyond its parts, contrary to an assumption. Third, he gives examples like spirit, motion, time, and place to show that we can know a word and its existence without conceiving its essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the three clarifications
Read each of the three 'I say' comments in the passage and their translations.
2
Understand each clarification
For each comment, identify what the author is clarifying about definition.
First comment: on mental transfer without prior knowledge. Second: on the whole vs. parts. Third: on examples.
3
Reflect on the examples
Consider the examples of spirit, motion, time, and place. How do they support the earlier argument?
These show that we can know a word and its existence without conceiving its essence.

الموصوف وعلى تصور كل ما عداه وذلك محال، أما الأول فلانه يلزم منه الدور وأما الثاني فلأنه يقتضى تقدم تصور جميع الماهيات التي لا نهاية لها على سبيل التفصيل (١) وأما تعريفها بما يتركب من الداخل والخارج فبطلان ما تقدم من الأقسام يقتضى بطلانه (٢). لا يقال : نحن نجد النفس طالبة لتصور ماهية الملك والروح في قولك فيه؟ لانا نقول : ذلك إما طلب تفسير اللفظ أو طلب البرهان على وجود المتصور وكلاهما تصديق (٣). أقول : تعريف الموصوف يتوقف على كون الوصف المعرف بحيث ينتقل الذهن من تصوره إلى تصور ماهية الموصوف لا على العلم يكون ذلك الوصف كذلك حتى يلزم المحال الذي ذكره، وأما كون الموصوف هو الموصوف بذلك الوصف دون كل ما عداه ليقن كون الوصف إما مساويا للموصوف وإما أخص منه والأول كالضاحك للإنسان والثاني كالكاتب له وعلى التقديرين يكون الوصف ملزوما والموصوف لازما ، واللزوم إن كان عقليا انتقل العقل من تصور الملزوم إلى تصور اللازم فيحصل التعريف ، ولا يكون العلم باللزوم شرطا في الانتقال ، فلا يلزم ذلك المحال . والتعريف في الأول يكون مطردا منعكسا وفي الثاني مطردا غير منعكس ، والذي ذكره من كون الوصف لازما للماهيات المختلفة على سبيل الاشتراك لا يحصل التعريف به ، وإن جعل معرفا كان للتعريف منعكسا غير مطرد (٢) أقول : هذا الكلام يقتضى وجوب كون كل واحد من أجزاء المعرف معرفا، وامتناع أن يكون للمجموع أثر غير ما يكون لكل واحد من أجزائه بطلانه ظاهر ، فإن قبل المجموع من حيث هو مجموع غير الأجزاء وهو خارج عن الماهية ، أديب بما مر من جواز كون الخارج معرفا . (٣) أقول : إنا نعرف تفسير لفظ الروح وتعلم يقينا وجوده في كل ذي روح ، ونجد العلماء يتخالفون في ماهيته كما سيذكره هو نفسه ، وليس ما يطلب منه أحد التصديقين اللذين ذكرهما وكذلك كثير من الأشياء تعلم تفسير لفظه ونحس بوجوده ، أو نعلم وجوده قطعا ويكون مع ذلك تصور ماهيته متعذرا على كثير من الناس كالحركة والزمان والمكان وغيرها .
The described (al-mawṣūf) and the conception of everything other than it, and that is impossible. As for the first, because it entails circularity (al-dawr); and as for the second, because it requires the prior conception of all infinite essences in detail (al-tafṣīl). (1) And as for defining it by what is composed of the internal and external, the falsity of the previous divisions necessitates its falsity. (2) It should not be said: we find the soul seeking the conception of the essence (māhiyya) of the angel and the spirit in your statement about it? For we say: that is either a request for explanation of the word (tafsīr al-lafẓ) or a request for proof (burhān) of the existence of the conceived, and both are assent (taṣdīq). (3) I say: The definition of the described depends on the describing term (al-waṣf al-muʿarraf) being such that the mind transfers from its conception to the conception of the essence of the described, not on the knowledge that that describing term is so, such that the impossibility he mentioned follows. As for the described being the one described by that describing term to the exclusion of everything else, because the describing term is either equivalent to the described or more specific. The first, like 'laughing' for man; the second, like 'writer' for him. In both cases, the describing term is a necessary concomitant (lāzim) and the described is a necessary concomitant (lāzim). If the concomitance (luzūm) is rational, the mind transfers from conception of the concomitant (malzūm) to conception of the necessary concomitant (lāzim), and definition occurs. Knowledge of the concomitance is not a condition for the transfer, so that impossibility does not follow. The definition in the first case is coextensive (muṭṭarid munʿakis) and in the second case coextensive but not converse (muṭṭarid ghayr munʿakis). What he mentioned—that the describing term is a necessary concomitant of different essences by way of common participation (ʿalā sabīl al-ishtirāk)—does not yield definition by it, and if it is made a definer, the definition would be converse but not coextensive. (2) I say: This speech necessitates that each part of the definer (al-muʿarraf) must be a definer, and the impossibility that the whole has an effect other than what each of its parts has is obviously false. For if the whole qua whole is other than the parts and is external to the essence (khārij ʿan al-māhiyya), it has been established by what preceded that it is permissible for the external to be a definer. (3) I say: We know the explanation of the word 'spirit' and we know with certainty its existence in every living thing, and we find scholars differing about its essence, as he himself will mention. And what is sought from one is neither of the two assents he mentioned. Likewise for many things: we know the explanation of their word and we sense their existence, or we know their existence with certainty, yet the conception of their essence is impossible for many people, like motion, time, place, and others.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is a proof-and-objection-answer unit concerning the impossibility of defining or conceiving everything. The author first argues that to define a thing by [its description] and by the conception of everything else is impossible for two reasons: it leads to circularity (dawr) or to an infinite regress of prior conceptions. Then he raises a potential objection: we find the soul seeking the conception of the essence of the angel and the spirit, implying that such conception is possible. The answer distinguishes between two types of seeking: either seeking the explanation of the word (tafsīr al-lafẓ) which is a kind of assent (taṣdīq) about meaning, or seeking proof of existence (burhān ʿalā wujūd al-mutaṣawwar) which is also assent. Thus the soul's search does not prove that conception is acquired from scratch; it is rather a request for definition or proof. The author then adds his own notes (أقول): in the first note, he clarifies that definition depends on the mind's transfer from the describing term to the essence, not on prior knowledge of that transfer, so the impossibility does not follow. He also distinguishes between equivalent and more specific describing terms and their logical properties. In the second note, he rejects the claim that each part of a definer must itself be a definer, because the whole qua whole is external to the parts and can be a definer. In the third note, he provides examples like 'spirit' and other concepts whose essence is unknown yet their word and existence are known, showing that the search for conception is not a search for a new simple conception but for explication.

المجموع

تفسير اللفظ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Each part of a definition must itself be a definer.
Correction: The author argues that the whole as a whole is external to the parts and can be a definer, so the parts do not individually need to be definers.

Checkpoint
According to the third clarification, what do we know about the spirit?
A) We know its essence but not its existence.
B) We know its word and its existence but not its essence.
C) We know neither its word nor its existence.
D) We know its essence and its existence.

Key Takeaway
Definition relies on mental transfer without prior knowledge, the whole can define beyond its parts, and many concepts are known by word and existence without essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite Allah’s names like Ar-Rahman, does your mind automatically transfer to His mercy, or do you merely utter the word without that mental shift? What would change if you deliberately sought that transfer in every prayer?
?
The lesson shows we can know a thing’s existence and its name without grasping its essence—like the soul. Are you at peace with not fully knowing Allah’s essence, or does it secretly trouble you? How does this shape your worship?
?
The author argues the whole can define even if its parts cannot. Think of a time you rejected a comprehensive Islamic teaching because one part confused you. Will you now hold back your judgment and embrace the whole, trusting that the whole’s meaning surpasses its parts?

The Four Sources of Human Conception
Imam Razi enumerates the four sources of human conception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four sources from which humans can conceive any idea, according to Imam Razi, and why induction confirms this limitation.

Core Idea
All human conception arises from sense, innate nature, intuition, or composition.

Imam Razi opens his note with a comprehensive statement about the limits of human conception. Before we can analyze how we know things, we must first understand the raw materials our minds have to work with. This passage identifies the four irreducible sources of conception and asserts that nothing else can be conceived.

Guided Path
1
Read the note carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's classification come through clearly.
The passage begins with a note (tanbīh) signaling an important clarification.
2
Identify the four sources
List the four distinct categories Razi mentions: sense perception, innate nature, intellectual intuition, and composition.
He gives examples for each: pain (innate), existence (intuition), mountain of ruby (composition).
3
Reflect on the role of induction
Razi says induction (istiqrāʾ) confirms that only these sources yield conception. Ask yourself what induction means here.
Induction surveys all known concepts and finds they fit these categories.
4
Test the claim
Think of a concept like 'justice' or 'triangle'. Which source does it come from? Can it be traced back to one of the four?
This will help internalize the classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه : ظهر لك أن الإنسان لا يمكنه أن يتصور إلا ما أدركه بحسه أو وجده في فطرة النفس كالألم واللذة أو من بديهة العقل كتصور الوجود والوحدة والكثرة أو ما يركبه العقل أو الخيال من هذه الأقسام ، فأما ما عداه فلا يتصوره البتة والاستقراء محققه. أقول ما يركبه الخيال كتصور جبل من ياقوت وإنسان يعاير وما يركبه العقل كالحيوان الناطق أو الموجود الواحد وما يركبانه معا كالسواد الواحد والحرارة الكلية والحدود مما ركبه العقل ، واعترف ههنا بتصور المركب الذي يركبه العقل ولا يراد بالتصور المكتسب غير نوع من ذلك فنقول ههنا مناقض لمذهبه في التصورات ، ثم إن أكثر الأجناس العالية مما لا يدرك بالحس ولا بالوجدان ولا بالبديهة ولا بالتركيب العقلي ، فإنها بسائط في العقل ، وقد يتصور بالرسوم وبتحليل ما يتصور عن أنواعها إليها.
Note (tanbīh): It has become clear to you that man cannot conceive except what he has perceived by his sense, or found in the innate nature (fiṭra) of the soul like pain and pleasure, or from the intuition (badīha) of the intellect like the conception of existence, oneness, and plurality, or what the intellect or imagination composes from these divisions. As for what is other than that, he never conceives it, and induction (al-istiqrāʾ) confirms it. I say: What the imagination composes like the conception of a mountain of ruby or a human flying [?] and what the intellect composes like 'rational animal' or 'one existent' and what both compose together like 'one blackness' and 'universal heat' and the definitions (ḥudūd) which the intellect composes. And here he acknowledges the conception of the composite which the intellect composes, and by acquired conception (al-taṣawwur al-muktasab) nothing other than a type of that is intended. So we say here it contradicts his doctrine on conceptions. Moreover, most of the highest genera (al-ajnās al-ʿāliya) are not perceived by sense, nor by inner sense (wijdān), nor by intuition, nor by rational composition, for they are simples (basāʾiṭ) in the intellect, and they may be conceived by descriptions (rusūm) and by analyzing what is conceived from their species into them.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is a note (tanbīh) summarizing what humans can conceive. The author states that conception is limited to: (1) what is perceived by the senses, (2) what is found in the innate nature of the soul (like pleasure and pain), (3) what is known by intellectual intuition (like existence, unity, multiplicity), and (4) what the intellect or imagination composes from these. Anything outside these categories cannot be conceived. He adds that induction confirms this. Then the author gives his own comment (أقول) expanding on the types of composition: imagination composes fanciful things like a mountain of ruby; intellect composes definitions like 'rational animal'; both together compose like 'one blackness'. He notes that the author of the main text acknowledges that the composite conceived by the intellect is a type of acquired conception, which seems to contradict the author's own earlier doctrine on conceptions. He further points out that many highest genera (like simple intelligibles) are not perceived by any of these means—they are intellectual simples—yet they can be conceived through descriptions and analysis of their species.

Key Terms
التصور
الحس
الفطرة
بديهة العقل
التركيب
الاستقراء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All knowledge comes from sense experience.
Correction: Razi includes innate nature and intellectual intuition as sources, not just sense.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, which of the following is NOT a source of conception?
A) Sense perception.
B) Innate nature of the soul.
C) Intellectual intuition.
D) Revelation.

Key Takeaway
Human conception is limited to what is perceived by sense, found in innate nature, known by intuition, or composed from these.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you last doubted a core belief about Allah, which of these four sources did you rely on to restore certainty—and which did you neglect? How will you use all four next time?
?
The source says anything outside these four is inconceivable. Think of a desire you have that contradicts this truth—perhaps a fantasy of something impossible. Are you willing to abandon that illusion and let your mind be limited to what Allah has made conceivable?
?
Imam Razi includes innate nature as a source of conception. What internal feeling or inclination have you been dismissing as 'just emotion' that might actually be a valid source of knowledge about yourself and your Creator?

Types of Composition and a Doctrinal Critique
Imam Razi's commentary on types of composition and a critique of the author's view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi distinguishes between compositions of the imagination and intellect, and why he finds a contradiction in the author's view on acquired conception.

Core Idea
Composition can be by imagination, intellect, or both, but a problem arises with 'acquired conception'.

After establishing the sources, Imam Razi adds a commentary to clarify the types of composition. He gives examples of what imagination and intellect each compose, and points out a contradiction in the author's earlier doctrine. This lesson unpacks that critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentary
Read the 'aqulu' section carefully. It is Razi's own addition to the main text.
The commentary starts with 'أقول'.
2
Distinguish imagination vs. intellect
Note the examples: imagination composes fantastic things like a mountain of ruby; intellect composes logical definitions like 'rational animal'.
Imagination ties to sensory images, intellect to abstract concepts.
3
Understand the shared composition
Both together compose concepts like 'one blackness' (unity + quality) and definitions.
This shows that composition is not purely one faculty.
4
Analyze the contradiction
Razi says the author acknowledges that the composite conceived by the intellect is a type of acquired conception, contradicting his earlier denial of such composites.
The earlier doctrine likely held that acquired conception is something else.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All composition is an intellectual act.

Checkpoint
What is an example used for composition by the intellect?
A) Mountain of ruby.
B) Rational animal.
C) One blackness.
D) Pain and pleasure.

Imagination and intellect compose concepts differently, and the idea of acquired conception must include these composites, causing a contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author of the text holds a contradiction between his definition of acquired conception and his admission here. Have you ever held two beliefs that logically contradict? What did you do when you realized it? Will you now examine your own beliefs for hidden contradictions and resolve them, even if it means changing a long-held view?
?
Imagination can create fanciful combinations like a mountain of ruby. Do you ever let your imagination lead you to false conclusions about your faith or about others? How can you train your mind to rely more on intellect and revelation than on unchecked imagination? Commit to one specific practice this week to discipline your imagination.
?
This lesson shows that even in precise scholarly discussions, contradictions can occur. When you encounter a difficulty in understanding a religious text, do you immediately assume the text is wrong, or do you re-examine your own assumptions? Write down one area where you might be too quick to judge and resolve to study it more deeply with humility.

Conceiving the Highest Genera
Imam Razi explains how the highest genera are conceived despite being simples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the highest genera (simple intelligibles) are conceived through descriptions and analysis of their species, despite not being directly perceived.

Core Idea
Highest genera are simples known through descriptions and species analysis.

Imam Razi adds a final point about the highest genera, which are simple concepts that cannot be broken down into the four sources. He explains that they are conceived through definitions (rusūm) and by analyzing the species that fall under them. This lesson explores that process.

Guided Path
1
Read the final part
Read from 'thumma' to the end. This is the concluding thought.
The passage starts with 'ثم إن'.
2
Identify the challenge
Highest genera are not perceived by any of the four sources: sense, inner sense, intuition, or composition.
They are simple (basāʾiṭ) in the intellect.
3
Understand the solution
They are conceived by descriptions (rusūm) and by analyzing what is conceived from their species into them.
For example, the genus 'substance' is known by describing it as 'that which exists not in a subject' and by analyzing particular substances.
4
Reflect on examples
Think of a highest genus like 'quality' or 'relation'. How would you describe it using the method of description and species analysis?
This will reinforce the point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Simple concepts are inconceivable.
Correction: They are conceived through descriptions and analysis of their species.

Checkpoint
How are the highest genera conceived according to the passage?
A) By direct sense perception.
B) By intuition.
C) By descriptions and analysis of species.
D) By rational composition.

Key Takeaway
The highest genera, though simple, are knowable through indirect means: definitions and species analysis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the most fundamental categories of existence—like 'substance' or 'existence'—do you rely on your senses or on careful analysis and description? How might this lesson challenge you to deepen your understanding of what you think you already know?
?
Consider a belief you hold that feels self-evident. Could it be that you only know it through descriptions and not direct intuition? How would that change your certainty?
?
The highest genera are simple yet known through analysis. What does that say about the humility required in seeking knowledge? Will you commit to analyzing your concepts rather than assuming you already fully grasp them?

The Necessity of Non-Acquired Conceptions
Imam Razi states the necessity of non-acquired conceptions and the rule for acquisition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why there must be non-acquired conceptions and the rule that determines which conceptions are acquired and which are not.

Core Idea
Not all conceptions can be acquired; some must be primitive to avoid infinite regress.

Imam Razi begins by establishing that those who allow acquired conception agree that it is not all-encompassing. He then provides a rule (ḍābiṭ) to distinguish between conceptions that must be non-acquired and those that may be acquired.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
Notice that if all conceptions were acquired, an infinite regress or circularity would result. This is impossible, so some conceptions must be non-acquired.
This is a foundational principle in logic to avoid infinite chains of dependence.
2
Understand the rule
The rule states: a conception on which a non-acquired assent depends must itself be non-acquired. However, a conception on which an acquired assent depends may be either acquired or not.
This distinguishes between necessarily primitive conceptions and those that can be obtained through definition.
3
Reflect on implications
Consider how this rule prevents circularity and provides a foundation for knowledge acquisition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تفريع : القائلون بأن التصور قد يكون كسبيا اتفقوا على أنه ليس كله كذلك وإلا لزم التسلسل أو الدور وهما محالان، بل لابد من تصورات غنية عن الاكتساب ، ثم الضابط أن كل تصور يتوقف عليه تصديق غير مكتسب فهو غير مكتسب، أما الذي يتوقف عليه تصديق مكتسب فقد يكون مكتسبا وقد لا يكون مكتسبا ، واتفقوا على أنه لا يمكن أن يكون المكتسب نفس المكتسب (1) بل إن كان مجموع أجزائه ، فهي الحد التام ، أو بعض أجزائه المساوية فهي الحد الناقص ، أو الأمر المخارج وحده وهو الرسم الناقص ، أو ما يتركب من الداخل والخارج وهو الرسم التام (۲) . أقول : قوله كل تصور يتوقف عليه تصديق غير مكتسب فهو غير مكتسب ، إنما يصح على مذهبه أن التصديق عبارة عن التصورات مع الحكم ، ولا يصح على قول من يقول إنه هو الحكم وحده ، فإن كثيرا من التصديقات البديهية أعني الأحكام المجردة عن التصورات تتوقف على تصورات غير بديهية كقولنا : كل عدد إما أول وإما مركب. (۲) أقول : المشهور عند الحكماء أن الرسم العام هو الذي يميز الشيء عن جميع ما عداه والرسم الناقص هو الذي يميزه عن بعض ما عداه ، واصطلاحه هنا بخلاف ذلك.
Subdivision (tafrīʿ): Those who say that conception may be acquired agree that it is not all acquired, otherwise an infinite regress (tasalsul) or circularity (dawr) would follow, both of which are impossible. Rather, there must be conceptions that are independent of acquisition. Then the rule (ḍābiṭ) is that every conception on which a non-acquired assent (taṣdīq) depends is itself non-acquired. As for that on which an acquired assent depends, it may be acquired or not acquired. And they agree that the acquired cannot be the very same as the acquired [reference unclear] (1). Rather, if it is the sum of its parts, that is the complete definition (al-ḥadd al-tāmm); or some of its equal parts, that is the incomplete definition (al-ḥadd al-nāqiṣ); or the differentia alone, that is the incomplete description (al-rasm al-nāqiṣ); or what is composed of the internal and external, that is the complete description (al-rasm al-tāmm) (2). I say: His statement 'every conception on which a non-acquired assent depends is non-acquired' is valid only on his doctrine that assent is conceptions with judgment, but not on the view that it is judgment alone, for many intuitive assents—i.e., judgments stripped of conceptions—depend on non-intuitive conceptions, as in our statement: 'Every number is either prime or composite.' (2) I say: The well-known view among philosophers is that the general description (al-rasm al-ʿāmm) is that which distinguishes a thing from everything else, and the incomplete description (al-rasm al-nāqiṣ) is that which distinguishes it from some other things, but his terminology here is contrary to that.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is a subdivision (tafrīʿ) concerning acquired conception and the types of definitions. The author first states that those who allow acquired conception agree that not all conceptions are acquired, otherwise infinite regress or circularity would result. Therefore, there must be non-acquired primitive conceptions. The rule is: a conception on which a non-acquired assent depends must itself be non-acquired. However, a conception on which an acquired assent depends may be either acquired or not. Then he enumerates four types of definitions used when a conception is acquired: (1) The sum of all parts of the definiendum—this is the complete definition (ḥadd tāmm); (2) some of its coextensive parts—incomplete definition (ḥadd nāqiṣ); (3) the differentia alone—incomplete description (rasm nāqiṣ); (4) what is composed of internal and external—complete description (rasm tāmm). In his first comment (أقول), the author notes that the rule about dependence holds only if one defines assent as conceptions plus judgment, not if assent is judgment alone, giving the example of mathematical propositions where the assent depends on conceptions that are not intuitive. In the second comment, he contrasts his usage of 'general description' and 'incomplete description' with the standard philosophical usage.

Key Terms
التصور الكسبي
التصديق
التسلسل
الدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all conceptions are acquired through learning.
Correction: The author shows that some conceptions must be non-acquired to avoid infinite regress or circularity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what would happen if all conceptions were acquired?
A) Infinite regress or circularity would follow.
B) Knowledge would be impossible.
C) All conceptions would be intuitive.
D) There would be no definitions.

Key Takeaway
Some conceptions must be non-acquired to prevent infinite regress or circularity, and the rule specifies which ones.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a new idea, do you assume it must be fully defined or proven? How does the necessity of primitive conceptions challenge your intellectual pride in deriving everything from reason alone?
?
Consider a foundational belief you hold — like the existence of God or the reliability of your senses. Is it acquired or non-acquired? If it is non-acquired, how does that affect your responsibility to examine it?
?
If some truths must be accepted without proof to avoid infinite regress, are you willing to embrace those truths with certainty? What would it cost you to reject them?

The Four Types of Definitions
Imam Razi enumerates the four types of definitions for acquired conception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four types of definitions: complete definition, incomplete definition, incomplete description, and complete description.

Core Idea
Acquired conceptions are defined by one of four types: complete or incomplete, definition or description.

After establishing the need for non-acquired conceptions, Imam Razi explains how acquired conceptions are attained through definitions. He enumerates four types based on whether the definition includes all parts, some equal parts, the differentia alone, or a combination of internal and external parts.

Guided Path
1
Recall that the acquired cannot be itself
The author states that the acquired conception cannot be the very same as the acquired—meaning a definition must be different from the definiendum.
This is a general principle in definition: the definiens should not be identical to the definiendum.
2
Identify the four types
1. Sum of all parts → complete definition (ḥadd tāmm). 2. Some equal parts → incomplete definition (ḥadd nāqiṣ). 3. Differentia alone → incomplete description (rasm nāqiṣ). 4. Compound of internal and external → complete description (rasm tāmm).
These types cover the ways a definition can capture the essence or distinguish the thing.
3
Understand the terminology
Note that 'definition' (ḥadd) refers to essence-revealing, while 'description' (rasm) refers to distinguishing through properties. Complete vs. incomplete refers to whether all essential parts are included.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تفريع : القائلون بأن التصور قد يكون كسبيا اتفقوا على أنه ليس كله كذلك وإلا لزم التسلسل أو الدور وهما محالان، بل لابد من تصورات غنية عن الاكتساب ، ثم الضابط أن كل تصور يتوقف عليه تصديق غير مكتسب فهو غير مكتسب، أما الذي يتوقف عليه تصديق مكتسب فقد يكون مكتسبا وقد لا يكون مكتسبا ، واتفقوا على أنه لا يمكن أن يكون المكتسب نفس المكتسب (1) بل إن كان مجموع أجزائه ، فهي الحد التام ، أو بعض أجزائه المساوية فهي الحد الناقص ، أو الأمر المخارج وحده وهو الرسم الناقص ، أو ما يتركب من الداخل والخارج وهو الرسم التام (۲) . أقول : قوله كل تصور يتوقف عليه تصديق غير مكتسب فهو غير مكتسب ، إنما يصح على مذهبه أن التصديق عبارة عن التصورات مع الحكم ، ولا يصح على قول من يقول إنه هو الحكم وحده ، فإن كثيرا من التصديقات البديهية أعني الأحكام المجردة عن التصورات تتوقف على تصورات غير بديهية كقولنا : كل عدد إما أول وإما مركب. Rather, if it is the sum of its parts, that is the complete definition (al-ḥadd al-tāmm); or some of its equal parts, that is the incomplete definition (al-ḥadd al-nāqiṣ); or the differentia alone, that is the incomplete description (al-rasm al-nāqiṣ); or what is composed of the internal and external, that is the complete description (al-rasm al-tāmm) (2). Commentary
This passage is a subdivision (tafrīʿ) concerning acquired conception and the types of definitions. The author first states that those who allow acquired conception agree that not all conceptions are acquired, otherwise infinite regress or circularity would result. Therefore, there must be non-acquired primitive conceptions. The rule is: a conception on which a non-acquired assent depends must itself be non-acquired. However, a conception on which an acquired assent depends may be either acquired or not. In the second comment, he contrasts his usage of 'general description' and 'incomplete description' with the standard philosophical usage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a definition always includes all essential parts.
Correction: There are also incomplete definitions and descriptions that include only some parts or differentia alone.

Checkpoint
What is the complete description (al-rasm al-tāmm) composed of?
A) Only the differentia.
B) Sum of all parts.
C) Internal and external components.
D) Some equal parts.

Key Takeaway
Acquired conceptions are defined by four types: complete/incomplete definition and complete/incomplete description, based on the parts included.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn a new concept in 'aqeedah, do you usually settle for a vague description (rasm) or do you seek a precise definition (ḥadd)? How has that habit either deepened or limited your understanding? Will you commit to asking 'What is the complete definition?' before moving on?
?
Think of a key concept you often explain to others—like tawḥīd or imān. Which of the four types of definitions do you typically use? Might an incomplete definition be causing confusion in those you teach? What would change if you refined it to a complete definition or description?
?
The Prophet ﷺ said, 'Whoever Allah wishes good for, He gives understanding of the religion.' Understanding requires precise definitions. Are you content with superficial grasp of important concepts? Will you now examine one core belief and work to define it using the four types, noting where your current understanding falls short?

The Author's Remarks on Assent and Terminology
Imam Razi adds two comments: one on the rule's dependence on the definition of assent, and another on terminological differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's two remarks: one about the rule's dependency on the definition of assent, and the other about the terminology of 'general description' and 'incomplete description'.

Core Idea
The rule about non-acquired conception depends on how assent is defined; and the author's terminology for description differs from the philosophers' standard.

Imam Razi adds two important clarifications. First, his rule that a conception on which a non-acquired assent depends is itself non-acquired only holds if assent is defined as conceptions plus judgment. If assent is mere judgment, the rule fails. Second, he notes that his usage of 'general description' and 'incomplete description' is contrary to the well-known philosophical usage.

Guided Path
1
First remark: the rule's conditional validity
The author comments that his earlier rule (every conception on which a non-acquired assent depends is non-acquired) is valid only if assent is defined as conceptions with judgment. If assent is judgment alone, then many intuitive assents may depend on non-intuitive conceptions.
He gives the example: 'Every number is either prime or composite' — the assent (judgment) is intuitive, but the conceptions of 'prime' and 'composite' are not intuitive.
2
Second remark: terminology discrepancy
The author points out that among philosophers (al-ḥukamā'), 'general description' (al-rasm al-ʿāmm) distinguishes a thing from everything else, and 'incomplete description' (al-rasm al-nāqiṣ) distinguishes from some others, but his own terminology in the passage is different.
This clarifies that the four types he listed use different definitions of 'complete' and 'incomplete' for description.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

According to the author, when does the rule about non-acquired conception apply?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you have a precise definition of 'assent' (تصديق) in your own heart when you say 'I believe' in Allah and His Messenger? Or have you been content with a vague feeling, never examining whether your assent includes clear conceptions and judgment? Will you now commit to knowing exactly what you are affirming?
?
The author warns that even a simple judgment like 'every number is prime or composite' depends on non-intuitive concepts. Think of a core Islamic belief you hold: Are you sure your understanding of its terms (e.g., 'Qadr', 'Risalah') is not as shallow as a person who says 'prime number' without truly knowing what it is? What will you do to deepen your conceptions so your assent is built on sound knowledge?
?
The author deliberately uses terminology differently from the philosophers. This shows that even scholars adjust language for precision. Have you ever misunderstood a scholar because you assumed his terms matched the common usage? How will you discipline yourself to read carefully and ask 'What does this author mean by this term?' before making judgments?

The Knowability of Simple and Composite Things
Imam Razi enumerates four cases of simple and composite things regarding knowability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four combinations of simple and composite things and their implications for definition.

Core Idea
Not everything can be defined or used to define—it depends on simplicity and composition.

Imam Razi presents a classification of things based on whether they are simple (not composed of parts) or composite, and whether they themselves are composed of others. This determines if a thing can be known (defined) and if it can serve to define other things. Understanding this helps avoid errors in constructing definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation once to get the overall structure. Note the four cases enumerated by Imam Razi.
2
Identify the four cases
Label each case: simple-not-composed-from, composite-composed-from, composite-not-composed-from, simple-composed-from. Understand each with examples.
The author gives examples: Necessary Existent (simple not composed), animal (composite composed?), etc.
3
Understand the pattern
Notice that a thing is known if it is composite (has parts), and can be used to know something else if it is composed of others (i.e., it has components that can be used to define other things).
This is a logical classification of definability.
4
Reflect on the Necessary Existent
Why is the Necessary Existent an example of the first case (simple not composed from)? Because it has no parts and nothing is prior to it, so it cannot be defined and cannot be used to define anything else.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تذنيبات (۱) البسيط الذي لا يتركب عنه غيره لا يعرف ولا يعرف به ، والمركب الذي يتركب عنه غيره يعرف ويعرف به ، والمركب الذي لا يتركب عنه غيره يعرف ولا يعرف به ، والبسيط الذي يتركب عنه غيره لا يعرف ويعرف به ، والمراد من هذه التعريفات الحدية (٣). ( ب ) يجب الاحتراز عن تعريف الشيء بما هو مثله وبالأخفى ، وعن تعريف الشيء بنفسه وبما لا يعرف إلا به ، إما بمرتبة واحدة أو بمراتب (4). أقول : يورد في أمثلتها واجب الوجود والحيوان والإنسان والجوهر . (٤) أقول : قيل في مثال تعريف الشيء بما هو مثله تعريف الزوج بأنه ليس بفرد ، وهذا بالحقيقة تعريف بما هو أخفى ، أو تعريف دوري لأن الإعدام تعرف بالملكات ، وههنا تفسير الفرد أنه ليس بمنقسم بعددين متساويين ، ومعناه أنه ليس بزوج فليس هذا التعريف بما هو مثله ، والمثال المطابق تعريف الاب بأن له ابن ، ويوردون في مثال التعريف بالأخفى تعريف النار بأنه استطقس شبيه بالنفس ، وفي تعريف الشيء بنفسه تعريف الإنسان بأنه حيوان بشري ، وبما لا يعرف إلا به بمرتبة تعريف الكيفية بما به تقع المشابهة ، وقد تعرف المشابهة بأنه اتفاق بالكيفية ، وفي ما لا يعرف إلا به مراتب تعريف الاثنين بأنه زوجا أول، والزوج يعرف بأنه منقسم بمتساويين ، والمتساويين بأنهما شيئان يلحقهما نوع واحد آخر من الكميات، ولا بد من أن تؤخذ الاثنينية في حد الشيئين .
Appendices (tadhyīlāt): (1) The simple (basīṭ) from which nothing else is composed is not known and is not known by [anything else]. The composite (murakkab) from which something else is composed is known and is known by [it]. The composite from which nothing else is composed is known and is not known by [it]. The simple from which something else is composed is not known and is known by [it]. And what is meant by these are the definitional definitions (al-taʿrīfāt al-ḥaddiyya) (3). (B) One must avoid defining a thing by what is like it and by what is more obscure, and avoid defining a thing by itself and by what is not known except through it, whether in one degree or in multiple degrees (4). I say: Examples of these are: the Necessary Existent, animal, man, and substance. (4) I say: It is said that an example of defining a thing by what is like it is defining 'even' (al-zawj) as 'not odd' (laysa bi-fard). But this is in reality a definition by what is more obscure, or a circular definition, because privations are known by the corresponding possessions. And here the explanation of 'odd' is 'that which is not divisible by two equal numbers', meaning it is 'not even', so this definition is not by what is like it. The correct example is defining 'father' as 'having a son'. And they give as an example of definition by what is more obscure: defining 'fire' as 'an element similar to the soul'. And as an example of defining a thing by itself: defining 'man' as 'rational animal' (but that is not self-defining). [Alternatively,] defining 'man' as 'human animal' (insān basharī). And as an example of defining by what is not known except through it in one degree: defining 'quality' (al-kayfiyya) as 'that by which similarity occurs', and similarity is defined as 'agreement in quality'. And for what is not known except through it in multiple degrees: defining 'two' as 'the first even number', and 'even' is defined as 'that which is divisible by two equals', and 'two equals' as 'two things that are affected by another single species of quantity', and it is necessary that 'twoness' be taken in the definition of 'two things'.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage presents two appendices (tadhyīlāt) on definitional rules. The first appendix distinguishes four cases of knowability based on simplicity and composition: (a) A simple thing from which nothing else is composed is neither known nor can it be used to know something else (i.e., it is indefinable and cannot serve as a definer). (b) A composite thing from which something else is composed is both known and can be used to know something else. (c) A composite thing from which nothing else is composed is known but cannot be used to know something else. (d) A simple thing from which something else is composed is not known but can be used to know something else. The author notes these are 'definitional definitions' (al-taʿrīfāt al-ḥaddiyya) and gives examples in his comment: the Necessary Existent (simple not composed of parts), animal (composite from which something else is composed?), etc. The second appendix lists four things to avoid in definitions: (1) defining a thing by what is like it, (2) by what is more obscure, (3) by itself, and (4) by what is not known except through it—whether in one or multiple degrees. In the lengthy comment, the author discusses examples for each fault, critically analyzing standard examples. For instance, defining 'even' as 'not odd' is actually a definition by what is more obscure because 'odd' is defined in terms of even, leading to circularity. The proper example of defining by like is 'father has a son'. He also discusses definitions by what is more obscure (fire as element similar to soul) and by itself (man as human animal), and by what is known only through the definiendum in one degree (quality and similarity) and in multiple degrees (two, even, two equals). He emphasizes that the definition of 'two' must include the notion of twoness in the definition of 'two things' to avoid circularity.

Key Terms
البسيط
المركب
الحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that simple things like the Necessary Existent can be defined using other terms.
Correction: The passage states that a simple from which nothing else is composed cannot be known (defined) nor used to define, because it has no parts and nothing prior.

Checkpoint
According to the first appendix, which type of thing can both be known and be used to know something else?
A) The simple from which nothing else is composed.
B) The composite from which something else is composed.
C) The composite from which nothing else is composed.
D) The simple from which something else is composed.

Key Takeaway
The knowability of a thing for definition depends on whether it is simple or composite and on its relational composition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Can you accept that the Necessary Existent—your Lord—cannot be defined by your finite mind? Does that unsettle you, or does it deepen your awe? How will you respond when your intellect demands definitions that God has not given?
?
Consider the fourth type: something indefinable yet used to define others. In your own convictions, are there core beliefs you hold that you cannot fully explain but that shape everything else? Are you willing to stand firm on them without complete rational clarity?
?
This lesson teaches that not all knowledge is definitional. How often do you dismiss an idea because you cannot 'put it in a box'? What would it mean for your faith to embrace mysteries that resist neat definition?

Four Faults to Avoid in Definition
Imam Razi lists four forbidden types of definitions and critiques examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will recognize the four types of faulty definitions: by like, by more obscure, by itself, and by what is only known through the definiendum.

Core Idea
A valid definition must not be circular, obscure, self-referential, or dependent on the very thing defined.

The second appendix lists four prohibitions in defining: defining a thing by something similar, by something more obscure, by itself, or by something only known through the defined thing. Imam Razi then critiques examples given by others and provides correct ones. This lesson explores these faults and their examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the main rule (the four prohibitions) and then the detailed commentary with examples.
2
Identify each fault
List the four faults: (1) by like (مثله), (2) by more obscure (أخفى), (3) by itself (بنفسه), (4) by what is only known through it (بما لا يعرف إلا به).
Note: 'by what is only known through it' can be in one degree or multiple degrees.
3
Examine the examples
For each fault, note the examples given and the criticism. For instance, defining 'even' as 'not odd' is actually a case of circularity/obscurity, not by like. The correct example of 'by like' is defining 'father' as 'having a son'.
The author also points out that defining 'man' as 'human animal' is a case of defining by itself.
4
Understand the chain in multiple degrees
The example of defining 'two' as 'first even' leads to a chain that eventually includes 'twoness' in the definition of 'two things', which is circular.
This shows how multiple degrees of dependence still constitute a fault.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is presented as a true example of defining a thing by what is like it?
A) Defining 'even' as 'not odd'.
B) Defining 'father' as 'having a son'.
C) Defining 'fire' as 'element similar to soul'.
D) Defining 'man' as 'rational animal'.

Key Takeaway
To avoid faulty definitions, ensure the definer is not similar to, more obscure than, identical to, or dependent on the definiendum.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you define your own identity — as a believer, a spouse, a parent — do you ever use circular definitions that depend on the very thing you are trying to understand? How can you break that cycle today?
?
Imagine you are asked to define 'justice' to someone who has never experienced it. Which of these four faults might you fall into without realizing? What specific step will you take to ensure your definition is clear and non-circular?
?
The lesson warns against defining by more obscure: using something less known to define something known. In your spiritual life, do you ever explain the known (like Allah's mercy) by something less known (like human love)? What would it cost you to instead use the Qur'anic definitions directly?

The Condition for the Rule of Unacquired Conceptions
The author clarifies a conditional rule about unacquired affirmations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the rule that every conception upon which an unacquired affirmation depends is unacquired is only valid if affirmation includes both conceptions and a judgment.

Core Idea
The rule about unacquired conceptions hinges on what we mean by 'affirmation'.

One of the foundational rules in kalam logic is that if a conception upon which an affirmation depends is itself unacquired, then the affirmation is unacquired. But this rule is not universally accepted; it depends on the definition of affirmation. The author clarifies that this rule only holds if affirmation is taken to include both the conceptions and the judgment. If affirmation is solely the judgment, then there are cases where self-evident affirmations depend on non-self-evident conceptions. Let's see how the author distinguishes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation of the first marginal comment. Note the author's conditional language.
The passage begins with 'I say: His statement ... is only valid according to his doctrine'.
2
Identify the two views on affirmation
The passage contrasts two views: affirmation as conceptions with judgment vs. affirmation as judgment alone.
3
Understand the example
The example 'every number is either prime or composite' shows a self-evident affirmation that depends on non-self-evident conceptions.
This example illustrates why the rule fails under the second view.
4
Connect to the rule
See how the rule applies only under one of the two views. The author uses this to show the dependence on definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(1) أقول : قوله كل تصور يتوقف عليه تصديق غير مكتسب فهو غير مكتسب ، إنما يصح على مذهبه أن التصديق عبارة عن التصورات مع الحكم ، ولا يصح على قول من يقول إنه هو الحكم وحده ، فإن كثيرا من التصديقات البديهية أغنى الأحكام المجودة عن التصورات تتوقف على تصورات غير بديهية كقولنا : كل عدد إما أول وإما مركب
(٢) أقول : المشهور عند الحكماء أن الرسم العام هو الذي يميز الشيء عن جميع ما عداه والرسم للناقص هو يميزه عن بعض ما عداه ، واصطلاحه هنا بخلاف ذلك
(۳) أقول : يورد فى أمثلتها واجب الوجود والحيوان والإنسان والجوهر .
(٤) أقول : قيل في مثال تعريف الشيء بما هو مثله تعريف الزوج بأنه ليس بفرد ، وهذا بالحقيقة تعريف بما هو أخفى ، أو تعريف دورى لأن الإعدام تعرف بالملكات ، وههنا تفسير الفرد أنه ليس بمنقسم بعددين متساويين ، ومعناه أنه ليس بزوج فليس هذا التعريف بما هو مثله ، والمثال المطابق تعريف الاب بان له ابن ، ويوردون فى مثال التعريف بالأخفى تعريف النار بأنه استطقس شبيه بالنفس ، وفي تعريف الشيء بنفسه تعريف الإنسان بأنه حيران بشرى ، وبما لا يعرفى إلا به بمرتبة تعريف الكيفية بما به تقع المشابهة ، وقد تعرف المشابهة بأنه اتفاق بالكيفية ، وفى ما لا يعرف إلا به مراتب تعريف الاثنين بأنه زوجا ج أول، والزوج يعرف بأنه منقسم بمتساويين ، والمتساويين بأنهما شيئان يلحقهما نوع واحد آخر من الكميات، ولا بد من أن تؤخذ الاثنينية في حد الشيئين .
(1) I say: His statement 'every conception upon which an unacquired affirmation depends is unacquired' is only valid according to his doctrine that affirmation consists of conceptions with a judgment, and is not valid according to those who say it is the judgment alone. For many self-evident affirmations that are independent of judgments found from conceptions depend on non-self-evident conceptions, like our statement: 'Every number is either prime or composite.' (2) I say: The well-known view among philosophers is that the general description is what distinguishes a thing from everything else, and the incomplete description distinguishes it from some others; but his terminology here is contrary to that. (3) I say: Examples of these are: the Necessary Existent, animal, human, and substance. (4) I say: It is said that an example of defining a thing by what is like it is defining 'even' as 'not odd'; but this is actually a definition by what is more obscure, or a circular definition, because privations are known through possessions. Here they explain 'odd' as 'not divisible into two equal numbers', so it means 'not even', so this is not a definition by what is like it. A correct example is defining 'father' as 'having a son'. They give as an example of definition by what is more obscure: defining 'fire' as 'an element similar to the soul'. As for defining a thing by itself, an example is defining 'human' as 'a rational animal'. As for defining by what is not known except through it, in one degree, an example is defining 'quality' as 'that by which similarity occurs', but similarity is defined as 'agreement in quality'. As for defining by what is not known except through it in degrees, an example is defining 'two' as 'an even prime', but 'even' is defined as 'divisible into two equals', and 'two equals' as 'two things that are followed by another type of quantity', and it is necessary that duality be taken in the definition of the two things.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage contains four marginal comments (taʿlīqāt) by the author on the preceding discussion of definitions. Comment (1) clarifies that the rule 'every conception upon which an unacquired affirmation depends is unacquired' only holds if one accepts that affirmation (tasdiq) includes both conceptions and a judgment. If one holds that affirmation is solely the judgment, then many self-evident affirmations (e.g., 'every number is either prime or composite') depend on conceptions that are not self-evident. Comment (2) notes that the terminology used by the author differs from the standard philosophical usage: here the 'general description' (al-rasm al-ʿāmm) is the one that distinguishes the thing from everything else, whereas the 'incomplete description' (al-rasm al-nāqiṣ) distinguishes it from some others. Comment (3) simply gives examples for the earlier list of definitions: Necessary Existent, animal, human, and substance. Comment (4) provides a detailed discussion of the rules against defining a thing by what is like it, by what is more obscure, by itself, or by what is not known except through it. The author gives examples of each violation and corrects common misconceptions, such as the claim that defining 'even' as 'not odd' is an example of defining by what is like it; he argues it is actually a definition by what is more obscure or circular. He then supplies proper examples for each type of invalid definition.

Key Terms
تصور (tasawwur)
تصديق (tasdiq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The rule that 'every conception upon which an unacquired affirmation depends is unacquired' is universally true regardless of how affirmation is defined.
Correction: The rule only holds if affirmation includes both conceptions and a judgment. If affirmation is just the judgment, then many self-evident affirmations depend on non-self-evident conceptions, as shown by the number example.

Checkpoint
According to marginal comment (1), the rule about unacquired conceptions depends on what view of affirmation?
A) That affirmation includes only the judgment
B) That affirmation includes conceptions with judgment
C) That affirmation is entirely self-evident
D) That affirmation is always acquired

Key Takeaway
The rule about unacquired affirmations is only valid if affirmation includes both conceptions and a judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a rule or principle as universally true, only to later realize it relied on a hidden assumption about definitions? How did that change your confidence in other beliefs?
?
When you affirm something as true, do you ever pause to examine whether your understanding of the key terms is complete? How might your certainty shift if you discovered your definitions were inadequate?
?
This lesson shows that a seemingly simple logical rule fails under a different definition. In your own life, are there beliefs you hold as absolutely certain that might collapse if you examined the definitions behind them? Are you willing to test them?
