Classification of Descriptions and Invalid Definitions
The author clarifies terminology of descriptions and lists examples of invalid definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's distinctions between general and incomplete descriptions and the various types of invalid definitions with correct examples.

Core Idea
Not every definition that looks valid actually is — watch out for circularity and obscurity.

In this set of marginal comments, the author addresses two main issues: the precise meaning of 'general description' (rasm ʿāmm) and 'incomplete description' (rasm nāqiṣ) in the context of this work, and the rules against defining a thing by what is like it, by what is more obscure, by itself, or by what is not known except through it. The author corrects common misapplications and provides proper examples. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for constructing valid definitions in kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passages
Read comments (2), (3), and (4) in Arabic and translation.
These comments are on the same page.
2
Note the difference in terminology
Comment (2) points out that the author uses 'general description' differently from the philosophers.
The well-known view among philosophers is that general description distinguishes from everything else, but the author's usage here is contrary.
3
Examine the examples
Comment (3) gives a list of examples: Necessary Existent, animal, human, substance. Comment (4) enumerates types of invalid definitions.
Comment (4) provides both incorrect and correct examples.
4
Analyze the correction
See why 'even is not odd' is not a definition by what is like it, but circular or obscure. The author explains the proper examples for each type.
For example, defining 'father' as 'having a son' is a correct definition by what is like it.
5
Summarize the rules
Conclude what makes a definition valid or invalid: avoid circularity, obscurity, self-definition, and dependence on what is not known.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(1) أقول : قوله كل تصور يتوقف عليه تصديق غير مكتسب فهو غير مكتسب ، إنما يصح على مذهبه أن التصديق عبارة عن التصورات مع الحكم ، ولا يصح على قول من يقول إنه هو الحكم وحده ، فإن كثيرا من التصديقات البديهية أغنى الأحكام المجودة عن التصورات تتوقف على تصورات غير بديهية كقولنا : كل عدد إما أول وإما مركب
(٢) أقول : المشهور عند الحكماء أن الرسم العام هو الذي يميز الشيء عن جميع ما عداه والرسم للناقص هو يميزه عن بعض ما عداه ، واصطلاحه هنا بخلاف ذلك
(۳) أقول : يورد فى أمثلتها واجب الوجود والحيوان والإنسان والجوهر .
(٤) أقول : قيل في مثال تعريف الشيء بما هو مثله تعريف الزوج بأنه ليس بفرد ، وهذا بالحقيقة تعريف بما هو أخفى ، أو تعريف دورى لأن الإعدام تعرف بالملكات ، وههنا تفسير الفرد أنه ليس بمنقسم بعددين متساويين ، ومعناه أنه ليس بزوج فليس هذا التعريف بما هو مثله ، والمثال المطابق تعريف الاب بان له ابن ، ويوردون فى مثال التعريف بالأخفى تعريف النار بأنه استطقس شبيه بالنفس ، وفي تعريف الشيء بنفسه تعريف الإنسان بأنه حيران بشرى ، وبما لا يعرفى إلا به بمرتبة تعريف الكيفية بما به تقع المشابهة ، وقد تعرف المشابهة بأنه اتفاق بالكيفية ، وفى ما لا يعرف إلا به مراتب تعريف الاثنين بأنه زوجا ج أول، والزوج يعرف بأنه منقسم بمتساويين ، والمتساويين بأنهما شيئان يلحقهما نوع واحد آخر من الكميات، ولا بد من أن تؤخذ الاثنينية في حد الشيئين .
(1) I say: His statement 'every conception upon which an unacquired affirmation depends is unacquired' is only valid according to his doctrine that affirmation consists of conceptions with a judgment, and is not valid according to those who say it is the judgment alone. For many self-evident affirmations that are independent of judgments found from conceptions depend on non-self-evident conceptions, like our statement: 'Every number is either prime or composite.' (2) I say: The well-known view among philosophers is that the general description is what distinguishes a thing from everything else, and the incomplete description distinguishes it from some others; but his terminology here is contrary to that. (3) I say: Examples of these are: the Necessary Existent, animal, human, and substance. (4) I say: It is said that an example of defining a thing by what is like it is defining 'even' as 'not odd'; but this is actually a definition by what is more obscure, or a circular definition, because privations are known through possessions. Here they explain 'odd' as 'not divisible into two equal numbers', so it means 'not even', so this is not a definition by what is like it. A correct example is defining 'father' as 'having a son'. They give as an example of definition by what is more obscure: defining 'fire' as 'an element similar to the soul'. As for defining a thing by itself, an example is defining 'human' as 'a rational animal'. As for defining by what is not known except through it, in one degree, an example is defining 'quality' as 'that by which similarity occurs', but similarity is defined as 'agreement in quality'. As for defining by what is not known except through it in degrees, an example is defining 'two' as 'an even prime', but 'even' is defined as 'divisible into two equals', and 'two equals' as 'two things that are followed by another type of quantity', and it is necessary that duality be taken in the definition of the two things.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage contains four marginal comments (taʿlīqāt) by the author on the preceding discussion of definitions. Comment (1) clarifies that the rule 'every conception upon which an unacquired affirmation depends is unacquired' only holds if one accepts that affirmation (tasdiq) includes both conceptions and a judgment. If one holds that affirmation is solely the judgment, then many self-evident affirmations (e.g., 'every number is either prime or composite') depend on conceptions that are not self-evident. Comment (2) notes that the terminology used by the author differs from the standard philosophical usage: here the 'general description' (al-rasm al-ʿāmm) is the one that distinguishes the thing from everything else, whereas the 'incomplete description' (al-rasm al-nāqiṣ) distinguishes it from some others. Comment (3) simply gives examples for the earlier list of definitions: Necessary Existent, animal, human, and substance. Comment (4) provides a detailed discussion of the rules against defining a thing by what is like it, by what is more obscure, by itself, or by what is not known except through it. The author gives examples of each violation and corrects common misconceptions, such as the claim that defining 'even' as 'not odd' is an example of defining by what is like it; he argues it is actually a definition by what is more obscure or circular. He then supplies proper examples for each type of invalid definition.

Key Terms
رسم عام (rasm ʿāmm)
رسم ناقص (rasm nāqiṣ)
تعريف بالمثل (taʿrīf bi’l-mithl)
تعريف بالأخفى (taʿrīf bi’l-akhfā)
تعريف دوري (taʿrīf dawrī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Defining 'even' as 'not odd' is a valid example of defining by what is like it.
Correction: It is actually a definition by what is more obscure or circular, because 'odd' is defined negatively in terms of 'even', and vice versa.

Checkpoint
What is the correct example of defining a thing by what is like it, according to comment (4)?
A) Defining 'even' as 'not odd'
B) Defining 'father' as 'having a son'
C) Defining 'fire' as 'an element similar to the soul'
D) Defining 'human' as 'a rational animal'

Key Takeaway
A valid definition must avoid circularity, obscurity, and using what is like it or what is not known except through it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe your own faith, do you ever rely on circular reasoning—defining one term by another that itself depends on the first? Search your heart: are there core beliefs you hold that you have never truly defined without falling into such a trap?
?
This lesson shows that even a seemingly clear definition like 'even is not odd' can be flawed. In your daily life, how often do you accept definitions of good and evil, right and wrong, without checking if they are based on what is more obscure or circular? Will you commit to questioning the foundations of your moral vocabulary this week?
?
The passage warns against defining a thing by what is not known except through it. Consider your understanding of Allah's attributes: do you define them in terms that are themselves only known through those attributes? How can you ensure your conception of Allah is not built on circular or obscure definitions? What step will you take today to purify your understanding?

The Rule of Priority in Complete Definitions
Author clarifies rule of priority in definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the principle of presenting the more general (genus) before the more specific (differentia) is obligatory only in complete definitions, not in other contexts.

Core Idea
Priority does not imply obligation except in complete definitions.

In this marginal comment, the author clarifies a logical rule about the order of presentation in definitions. He argues that the claim that the more important must always come first is not generally valid—it only applies to complete definitions. This lesson explains why.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses a concise, argumentative style.
2
Identify the main claim
The author says priority does not entail obligation nor negate it. Find the key statement and understand what he is rejecting.
3
Understand the exception for complete definitions
Focus on the phrase 'only in complete definitions'. Why is priority required there? Because the genus is the more important part and must come first to avoid corrupting the formal part.
A complete definition has both genus and differentia in proper order.
4
Note the rule for other definitions
In definitions that are not complete, presenting the more known is preferable but not obligatory. This is a nuance often overlooked.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(1) أقول : الأولوية لا توجب الوجوب ولا تنافيه ، فدليله غير مثبت لدعواه ، وإنما يجب تقديم الأهم في الحدود النامة لا غير ؛ لأن الأهم فيها هو الجنس، وهو يدل على شيء مبهم يحصله الأخص الذي هو الفصل، و من تقديم الأخص على الأعم يختل الجزء الصورى من الحد فلا يكون تاما مشتملا على جميع الأجزاء، أما في غير الحد التام فتقديم الأعرف أولى وليس بواجب .
(٢) أقول : الحس إدراك ما له لون فقط والحكم تأليف بين مدركات بالحس أو بغير الحس على وجه يعرض المؤلف لذاته ، أما الصدق أو الكذب واليقين حكم ثان على الحكم الأول بالصدق على وجه لا يمكن أن يزول، وليس من شأن الحس التأليف الحكمي، لأنه إدراك فقط ، فلا شيء من الأحكام محسوسة أصلا ، فإذا كل ما هو محسوس لا يمكن أن يوصف من حيث كونه محسوسا بكونه يقينيا أو غير يقينى أو حقا أو باطلا أو صوابا أو غلطا ، فإن جميع هذه الأوصاف من لواحق الأحكام ، اللهم إلا إذا قارن المحسوس حكم غير مأخوذ من الحس وحينئذ يوصف بهذه الأوصاف من حيث كونه حكما ويقال له حكم حسى بقينى ، وإذا تقرر هذا ثبت أن المحسوس فى قوله إن اليقينيات هى المعقولات لا المحسوسات ليست بمحسوسات فقط ، وإنها لا يمكن أن تكون يقينية ولا غير يقينية بمعنى عدم الملكة ، بل إنما هى ليست بيقينية بمعنى السلب ، كما الإدراك وحده ليس حكما إذا كانت المحسوسات فى هذا الكلام مقارنة باعتبار كونه مطابقة أو غير مطابقة صوايا أو غلطا ، فادعاء أن الجماعة المذكورة من الحكماء زعموا أن المحسوسات لا تكون يقينية ليس بحق ، وذلك أن الحكماء ذكروا أن مبادى اليقينيات هى الأوليات والمحسوسات والمجربات والمتواترات ، والحدسيات وسموها بالقضايا الواجب قبولها ، وذكروا أن مبادى. المجربات والمتوازات والحدسيات هي الإحساس بالجزئيات ، وإن الأوليات يكتسبها الصبيان باستعداد يحصل لعقولهم من الإحساس بالجزئيات ، ولذلك حكم كبير الجماعة بأن من فقد حدا فقد علما، وأن أصول أكثر العلم الطبيعي كالعلم بالسماء
(1) I say: Priority does not entail obligation nor does it preclude it, so his evidence does not prove his claim. Rather, the more important must be presented only in complete definitions, because the more important in them is the genus, which indicates something vague that is specified by the more specific which is the differentia. If the more specific were presented before the more general, the formal part of the definition would be corrupted, so it would not be complete, containing all parts. As for other than complete definitions, presenting the more known is preferable but not obligatory. (2) I say: Sense is perception of what has color only, and judgment is a composition between percepts, by sense or otherwise, in a way that occurs to the composer itself. Truth or falsehood and certainty are a second judgment upon the first judgment with truth in a way that cannot be removed. It is not the function of sense to compose judgments, because it is only perception. Hence, none of the judgments are sense-perceptible at all. Therefore, everything that is sense-perceptible cannot be described in terms of being sense-perceptible as certain or uncertain, true or false, correct or incorrect, because all these attributes are accidents of judgments. Unless the sense-perceptible is accompanied by a judgment not derived from the sense, then it is described by these attributes as a judgment and is called a certain sense-based judgment. Given this, it is established that the sense-perceptible in the statement 'Certainties are intelligibles, not sensibles' is not merely sensibles, and they cannot be certain or uncertain in the sense of lacking the faculty, but rather they are not certain in the sense of negation, just as perception alone is not a judgment. When sense-perceptibles in this statement are considered in terms of being matching or non-matching, correct or incorrect, the claim that the aforementioned group of philosophers claimed that sense-perceptibles are not certain is not true. This is because the philosophers mentioned that the principles of certainties are primary propositions, sensibles, empirics, recurrent reports, and intuitives, which they called 'propositions that must be accepted'. They stated that the principles of empirics, recurrent reports, and intuitives are sense perception of particulars, and that children acquire primary propositions through a preparedness that occurs in their minds from sense perception of particulars. Hence, a great number of the community judged that whoever loses a sense loses knowledge, and that the foundations of most natural science, like knowledge of the sky...
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage contains two marginal comments on the discussion of affirmations. Comment (1) addresses the rule about presenting the more general before the more specific. The author argues that priority (al-awlawiyya) does not entail obligation (wujūb) nor negate it. The claim that the more general must be presented first is only required in complete definitions (ḥadd tāmm), because the genus is the more important part; if the specific were placed first, the formal part would be corrupted. In other than complete definitions, presenting the more known is preferable but not obligatory. Comment (2) is a lengthy analysis of the nature of sense perception and judgment. The author defines sense (ḥiss) as perception of what has color only (i.e., the external sense), while judgment is a composition of percepts. Truth, falsehood, and certainty are attributes of judgments, not of sense perceptions directly. Therefore, sense perceptions alone cannot be described as certain or uncertain. However, when a sense perception is accompanied by a judgment not derived from sense, it can be called a certain sense-based judgment. The author then applies this to the argument of the second group (who claimed certainties are intelligibles, not sensibles). He argues that this claim misunderstood that sense-perceptibles are not sensibles in the same sense; they are not certain in the sense of lacking the faculty, but rather they are not certain in the sense of negation (i.e., the attribute simply does not apply). He defends the philosophers (like Plato, etc.) by noting that they actually count sensibles among the principles of certainties, along with primary propositions, empirics, recurrent reports, and intuitives. He explains that empirics, recurrent reports, and intuitives are based on sense perception of particulars, and even primary propositions are acquired by children through sense perception of particulars. He concludes by noting that according to the majority, losing a sense leads to losing knowledge, and that the foundations of natural science (like knowledge of the sky) depend on sense.

Key Terms
أولوية (awlawiyya)
حد تام (ḥadd tāmm)
جنس (jins)
فصل (faṣl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Priority of the more general over the more specific is always obligatory.
Correction: It is only obligatory in complete definitions; in other definitions it is preferable but not obligatory.

Checkpoint
According to the author, when is presenting the more important (the genus) obligatory?
A) In all definitions
B) Only in complete definitions
C) Only in incomplete definitions
D) Never

Key Takeaway
Priority of genus over differentia is required only in complete definitions to maintain the formal part; elsewhere it is merely preferable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you argue for a belief, do you insist on a particular order of presentation as though it were obligatory, even when it is merely preferable? Where might you be forcing a strict priority that is not necessary?
?
In your understanding of core Islamic tenets, have you ever placed a less essential element before a more essential one, corrupting your definition? What can you do today to restore the proper order in your understanding?
?
The author distinguishes between necessary and preferred order in definitions. In your own life, are you treating matters of secondary importance as though they were fundamental, or neglecting fundamentals for the sake of convenience? How can you realign your priorities?

Sense, Judgment, and the Certainty of Sensibles
Author analyzes sense, judgment, and the certainty of sensibles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between sense perception and judgment, and how certainty is attributed only to judgments, not to raw sense data.

Core Idea
Certainty is a property of judgments, not of sense perceptions.

The second marginal comment tackles a deeper epistemological issue: can sense-perceptibles be considered certain? The author argues that sense perception alone is not judgment, so certainty cannot be directly attributed to it. However, when accompanied by a judgment, sense-based knowledge can be certain. He also defends the philosophers who listed sensibles among the principles of certainties.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, noting the definitions of sense and judgment. Then read the translation carefully.
The passage is long and argumentative; take your time.
2
Distinguish sense from judgment
The author defines sense as perception of color only, and judgment as composition of percepts. Note that judgment involves an additional act beyond perception.
3
Understand why sense alone cannot be certain
Certainty is a second judgment about the truth of a first judgment. Since sense perception is not a judgment, it cannot be described as certain or uncertain in itself.
The author says: 'none of the judgments are sense-perceptible at all.'
4
Examine the exception for sense-based judgments
When a judgment accompanies a sense perception (e.g., 'I see red'), the resulting composite can be called a certain sense-based judgment.
5
See how the philosophers include sensibles in certainties
The author defends the philosophers by showing that they listed sensibles among the principles of certainties (along with primary propositions, empirics, etc.). He explains that even primary propositions are acquired through sense perception of particulars.
This counters the claim that sensibles are not certain at all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

A) Perception of sounds
B) Perception of what has color only
C) Perception of all sensible qualities
D) Composition of judgments

Key Takeaway
Sense perception provides raw data, but certainty and truth are attributes of judgments; the philosophers correctly included sensibles among the sources of certainties when accompanied by judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you trust your senses, do you ever pause to consider whether you are making an unexamined judgment rather than simply perceiving? How often do you second-guess a sense perception by asking 'Is this really certain?'
?
The author says certainty is a second judgment that cannot be removed. Think of a belief you hold that you consider certain. Can you trace its origin back to a judgment you made, or did you inherit it uncritically? What would it take for that certainty to be genuinely unshakeable?
?
The philosophers linked losing a sense to losing knowledge. If you lost one of your senses today, what knowledge would you lose? How does this realization challenge your dependence on sense data and compel you to seek knowledge that transcends the senses?

The First Argument: Conceiving Non-Existence is Impossible
Razi presents the first proof that conceiving non-existence is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical steps in Razi's first argument that conceiving non-existence is impossible because it lacks distinction and establishment.

Core Idea
What is not established cannot be conceived.

Razi begins his discussion on the possibility of judging existence with a powerful argument. He shows that any judgment about existence depends on first conceiving non-existence. But can non-existence be conceived? Razi argues that it cannot, because conception requires the thing to be distinct and established in some way. This lesson walks through his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the tight logical chain: judgment -> conception -> distinction -> determination -> establishment.
2
Identify the key terms
Find the terms: تصديق (judgment), تصور (conception), عدم (non-existence), ثابت (established). Understand how they connect.
These are essential for the argument.
3
Trace the logic
Follow the reasoning: The judgment depends on conceiving non-existence. Conception requires distinction from others, which implies determination in itself, which implies establishment. Therefore, only what is established can be conceived. Non-existence is not established, so it cannot be conceived.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Since the conception is impossible, the judgment that depends on it is also impossible. Ask yourself: what does this mean for the broader discussion?
This argument sets up the need for the objections and responses that follow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدها : أن هذا التصديق موقوف على تصور أصل العدم والناس قد تحيروا فيه لأن المتصور لا بد وأن يتميز عن غيره والتميز عن غيره متعين فى نفسه ، وكل متعين فى نفسه فهو ثابت فى نفسه ، فكل متصور ثابت فى نفسه ، فما ليس بثابت فغير متصور فالمعدوم غير ثابت فلا يكون متصورا وإذا كان ذلك التصديق متفرعا على هذا التصور وكان هذا التصور ممتنعا كان ذلك التصديق ممتنعا . أقول : الي هو رفع الإثبات ورفع الإثبات لا يكون عين الإثبات ورفع الإثبات الخارجي إثبات ذهني منسوب إلى لا إثبات خارجي، وكونه فى الذهن متصورا ومتميزا عن غيره ومتعينا في نفسه وثابتا في الذهن لا ينافي كون ما هو منسوبا إليه لا ثابتا في الخارج ، فالحكم بأن ما ليس بثابت في الخارج غير متصور مطلقا باطل، لأنه متصور من حيث إنه ليس بثابت فى الخارج غير متصور لا من حيث هذا الوصف وذلك التصديق موقوف على هذا التصور من هذا الحيثية لا على ما نسب إليه هذا الوصف فلذلك لم يكن متنها. لا يقال : المعدوم المتصور له ثبوت في الذهن ، ولأن قولنا المعدوم غير متصور حكم على المعدوم بأنه غير متصور ، والحكم على الشيء يستدعى كون المحكوم عليه متصورا فلو لم يكن المعدوم متصورا لامتنع الحكم عليه بأنه غير متصور ، لانا نجيب عن الأول : بأن الثابت فى الذهن أحد أقسام مطلق الثابت والكلام وقع في تصورها مقابل مطلق الثابت وذلك المقابل يستحيل أن يكون ثابتا بوجه ما وإلا لكان داخلا تحت مطلق الثابت ، وحينئذ لا يكون قسيما له بل قسما منه ، وعن الثاني أن ما ذكرته ليس جوابا عن دليلنا ، على أن المعدوم غير متصور بل هو إقامة دليل ابتداء على أن المعدوم متصور ، وذلك يقتضى معارضة دليلين قاطعين في مسألة واحدة وهو أحد الدلائل القادحة في البديهيات . أقول : رفع الثبوت الشامل للخارجي والذهنى تصور لما ليس بثابت ولا متصور أصلا فيصح الحكم عليه من حيث هو ذلك النصور ولا يصح من حيث هو ليس بثابت ولا يكون تناقضا لاختلاف الموضوعين ولا مانع من أن يكون شيء قسما لشيء باعتبار وقسما منه باعتبار ، مثلا إذا قلنا الموجود إما ثابت في الذهن وإما غير ثابت في الذهن ، فالموجود من حيث له مفهوم قسم من الثابت في الذهن فإذا قد انحل الشك تعارض دليلين.
One of them: that this judgment (tasdiq) depends on conceiving (tasawwur) the principle of non-existence (al-`adam), and people have been perplexed about it, because what is conceived must necessarily be distinguished from others, and distinction from others is determined in itself, and everything determined in itself is established (thabit) in itself, so everything conceived is established in itself; so what is not established is not conceived, so non-existence is not established, so it cannot be conceived. And if that judgment is derived from this conception, and this conception is impossible, then that judgment is impossible. I say: it is the negation of affirmation, and the negation of affirmation is not the same as affirmation, and the negation of external affirmation is a mental affirmation attributed to non-external affirmation; and its being in the mind as conceived, distinguished from others, determined in itself, and established in the mind does not contradict that what it is attributed to is not established externally. So the judgment that what is not established externally is absolutely not conceived is false, because it is conceived insofar as it is not established externally – it is not conceived from this description itself; and that judgment depends on this conception from this aspect, not on what this description is attributed to, so it is not contradictory. It should not be said: the conceived non-existent has establishment in the mind. And because our statement 'non-existence is not conceived' is a judgment about non-existence that it is not conceived, and judgment about a thing requires that the thing judged be conceived; so if non-existence were not conceived, the judgment that it is not conceived would be impossible. We answer the first: that what is established in the mind is one of the divisions of absolute established (muthlaq al-thabit), and the discussion concerns its conception as opposite to absolute established, and that opposite is impossible to be established in any way, otherwise it would be included under absolute established, and then it would not be a partition of it but a part of it. And regarding the second: what you mentioned is not an answer to our proof that non-existence is not conceived; rather it is an initial proof that non-existence is conceived, and that requires the conflict of two decisive proofs in one issue, and that is one of the proofs that undermine the self-evident truths. I say: the removal of establishment in the inclusive sense (external and mental) is a conception of what is not established and not conceived at all, so the judgment about it is valid from the perspective that it is that conception, and not valid from the perspective that it is non-established, and there is no contradiction due to the difference of subjects. And there is no impediment to a thing being a partition of something in one respect and a part of it in another respect. For example, when we say: the existent is either established in the mind or not established in the mind, so the existent insofar as it has a concept is a part of the established in the mind; thus the doubt [of conflicting proofs] is resolved.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument (aḥaduhā) against the possibility of the judgment of existence/non-existence. It begins by stating that this judgment (al-taṣdīq) depends on conceiving (taṣawwur) non-existence (al-ʿadam). The argument then reasons that what is conceived must be distinguished from others, and this distinction implies that the conceived thing is determined in itself (mutaʿayyin fī nafsihī), and what is determined is established (thābit). Therefore, anything conceived is established. Since non-existence is not established, it cannot be conceived. If the judgment depends on an impossible conception, the judgment itself is impossible. The author (Imam Razi) then interjects with an 'I say' (أقول) clarifying that the conception in question is a mental concept, not an external reality; the object conceived is non-existence as a mental concept, which does not require external establishment. He refutes the objection that the concept of non-existence is established in the mind by distinguishing between absolute establishment and its subdivisions. The objection itself tries to prove that non-existence must be conceivable because the judgment 'non-existence is not conceived' presupposes its conception. The author responds that this is not an answer but a counter-proof leading to a conflict of two decisive proofs, which is a problem for self-evident truths. He concludes that the removal of inclusive establishment (removing both external and mental establishment) is itself a conception of what is not established at all, and the judgment can be made from that perspective without contradiction, using an example of dividing existent into mental and non-mental establishment.

Key Terms
تصديق
تصور
عدم
ثابت
متعين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that non-existence can be conceived because we talk about it, but Razi shows that the concept of non-existence, if it were truly conceived, would require it to be established, which contradicts its nature.
Correction: The argument shows that non-existence cannot be conceived because conception requires a form of establishment, which non-existence lacks.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's first argument, why is non-existence inconceivable?
A) Because non-existence is nothing, and nothing cannot be thought.
B) Because conception requires the object to be established, but non-existence is not established.
C) Because the word 'non-existence' has no meaning.
D) Because non-existence contradicts itself.

Key Takeaway
Razi proves that non-existence cannot be conceived because conception requires the object to be established in some way.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you try to think of 'nothingness,' do you catch yourself imagining an empty space or a void? What does this argument reveal about the hidden assumption in that act?
?
If conception of non-existence is impossible, then every thought you have is about something that is 'established' in some way. How does that challenge the way you dismiss certain ideas as 'mere illusions'?
?
This argument depends on the principle that what is conceived must be distinct. Are you willing to accept that your mind cannot genuinely entertain the concept of absolute non-existence? What does that imply about the foundation of your belief in a Creator who exists necessarily?

Responses to Objections: Mental Existence and the Self-Reference Paradox
Razi responds to objections, clarifying mental existence and resolving paradoxes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi responds to objections against his first argument, clarifying the nature of mental existence and resolving the paradox of judging non-existence.

Core Idea
Non-existence is conceived as a mental concept, not as an external reality.

Razi's first argument that non-existence is inconceivable faces two strong objections: first, that the concept of non-existence has mental establishment, and second, that the very judgment that non-existence is not conceived presupposes its conception. In this passage, Razi provides a nuanced response, distinguishing between external and mental existence, and showing how the objections miss the mark.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Razi's initial response
Start with the 'I say' (أقول) section. Notice how Razi distinguishes between external affirmation and mental affirmation.
This is a crucial distinction in kalam.
2
Understand the two objections
Identify the two objections: (1) the conceived non-existent has mental establishment, (2) the judgment that non-existence is not conceived requires that non-existence be conceived. Read them carefully.
3
Analyze Razi's answers
For objection 1, Razi explains that the concept of non-existence is established in the mind, but that does not make it established externally. For objection 2, he shows that it is a counter-proof, not an answer, leading to a conflict of decisive proofs.
4
Examine the resolution
Read the final 'I say' where Razi resolves the conflict by noting that 'removal of establishment' itself is a concept that can be judged from different perspectives without contradiction.
The example of existent divided into mental and non-mental establishment clarifies the point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument (aḥaduhā) against the possibility of the judgment of existence/non-existence. It begins by stating that this judgment (al-taṣdīq) depends on conceiving (taṣawwur) non-existence (al-ʿadam). He refutes the objection that the concept of non-existence is established in the mind by distinguishing between absolute establishment and its subdivisions. The objection itself tries to prove that non-existence must be conceivable because the judgment 'non-existence is not conceived' presupposes its conception. The author responds that this is not an answer but a counter-proof leading to a conflict of two decisive proofs, which is a problem for self-evident truths. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the objection about self-reference is a devastating refutation, but Razi shows it is a separate proof that leads to a conflict, not a direct answer.

How does Razi answer the objection that the concept of non-existence has mental establishment?
A) He denies that the concept of non-existence is in the mind.

C) He accepts the objection and modifies his argument.

Razi resolves the objections by distinguishing between external and mental existence and between different perspectives of judgment, showing that no contradiction remains.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that we can conceive of non-existence precisely as non-existence. Think of something you deeply fear losing — a possession, a relationship, or even your own life. That thing currently exists externally. If it ceased to exist, it would still exist as a concept in your mind. How does this distinction between external and mental existence affect your attachment to it? What concrete step can you take today to rely more on the eternal rather than the temporal?
?
The passage resolves a paradox by distinguishing between different perspectives of judgment. In your own spiritual life, where might you be confusing your mental conception of something (e.g., your self-image, your understanding of Allah) with its external reality? What is one assumption you hold that needs to be realigned, and what action will you take to check your mental conception against the truth?

The Second Argument: Distinction Requires Identity
Razi presents the second argument: distinction from existence requires non-existence to have an identity, leading to a vicious circle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument that conceiving non-existence leads to a vicious circle (khalf) because distinction requires identity, and why this is problematic.

Core Idea
To be distinct from existence, non-existence must have an identity — but that leads to a circular paradox.

Imam al-Razi presents a second argument against the conceivability of non-existence. Even if we grant that non-existence can be conceived, the very act of distinguishing it from existence requires it to have an identity. Razi then shows how this assumption leads to an infinite regress or absurdity, specifically a vicious circle (khalf). Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise about distinction
The argument begins by stating that if non-existence is conceivable, then the statement 'negation and affirmation do not combine' requires non-existence to be distinct from existence. This distinction presupposes that non-existence has an identity (huwiyya).
Focus on the logical flow: conceivability → distinction → identity.
2
See the implication of identity
Razi argues that every identity can be removed by the intellect; otherwise it would have no opposite. Therefore, the identity of non-existence can be removed, leading to a specific removal that is itself absolute non-existence.
This step shows why the premise leads to a problem.
3
Grasp the vicious circle conclusion
Because the removal of the identity of non-existence becomes a part of non-existence (a specific removal), it makes a division of non-existence equal to the whole − a vicious circle (khalf).
The argument concludes that the assumption leads to logical absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيها : لو سلمنا إمكان تصور العدم لكان قولنا النفى والإثبات لا يجتمعان يستدعى امتياز العدم الوجود ، وامتياز العدم عن الوجود يستدعى أن يكون لمسمى العدم هوية متميزة عن الوجود لكن ذلك محال ، لان كل هوية يشير العقل إليها و العقل يمكنه رفعها ، وإلا لم يكن له مقابل ، وكان يلزم أن لا يكون للعدم مقابل وكان يلزم نفى الوجود وهو باطل، فثبت أن ارتفاع الهوية المسماة بالعدم معقول ، لكر ارتفاع تلك الهوية ارتفاع خاص فيكون دا - لا النحت العدم المطلق ، فيكون قسم العدم قسما منه هذا خلف . أقول : الحكم بأن الامتياز يستدعى أن يكون للمتمايزين هويتان غير مسلم ، فإن الهوية واللاهوية ممتازان وليس اللاهوية هوية، ولو فرضنا لها هوية كانت بذلك الاعتبار داخلة في قسم الهوية وباعتبار ما فرض له هذا الاعتبار قسما للهوية ، وكذلك القول في رفع العدم ولا يلزم المخلف .
And the second: if we grant the possibility of conceiving non-existence, then our statement 'negation and affirmation do not combine' would require the distinction of non-existence from existence, and the distinction of non-existence from existence requires that the named non-existence have an identity (huwiyya) distinct from existence, but that is impossible, because every identity that the intellect points to, the intellect can remove it, otherwise it would have no opposite, and it would necessitate that non-existence have no opposite and necessitate the negation of existence, which is false. So it is established that the removal of the identity called non-existence is conceivable, because the removal of that identity is a specific removal, so it becomes the reality of absolute non-existence, so a division of non-existence becomes a part of it – that is a vicious circle (khalf). I say: the judgment that distinction requires that the two distinct things have two identities is not granted, for identity (huwiyya) and non-identity (lāhuwiyya) are distinct, and non-identity is not an identity; and if we assume it has an identity, it would by that consideration be included under the division of identity, and by the consideration of what is assumed for this consideration, it would be a partition of identity. Likewise for the removal of non-existence, and the vicious circle does not follow.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument (thāniyahā): even if we grant the conceivability of non-existence, the statement 'negation and affirmation do not combine' requires distinguishing non-existence from existence. This distinction presupposes that non-existence has an identity (huwiyya) distinct from existence. However, every identity can be removed by the intellect; otherwise it would have no opposite, leading to the negation of existence. Thus, the removal of that identity is conceivable, meaning that the removal itself becomes a specific removal that is absolute non-existence, causing a part of non-existence to be equal to the whole – a vicious circle (khalf). Razi refutes this by denying that distinction necessarily implies two distinct identities: identity and non-identity are distinct, but non-identity is not an identity. If one posits an identity for non-identity, it becomes a division of identity itself, not a separate thing. Thus, the regress is avoided.

Key Terms
امتياز
هوية
خلف
رفع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if non-existence is conceivable, it must be a positive entity with an identity distinct from existence.
Correction: The argument shows that assuming non-existence has an identity leads to a vicious circle, so it cannot be coherently conceived as such.

Checkpoint
What does the second argument claim must follow from the conceivability of non-existence?
A) Non-existence must have an identity distinct from existence.
B) Non-existence is identical to existence.
C) The intellect cannot remove the identity of non-existence.
D) Non-existence is a species of existence.

Key Takeaway
The second argument shows that if non-existence were conceivable as distinct from existence, it would have to have an identity, leading to a vicious circle (khalf).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If non-existence cannot truly be conceived as a distinct reality, what does that mean for your fear of nothingness or death? Where do you place your trust when your mind cannot grasp the opposite of existence?
?
Razi argues that every identity can be removed. Is there any 'identity' you cling to — your status, your possessions, your sense of self — that you assume is fixed? What would it mean for your reliance on Allah if you truly accepted that nothing besides Him has necessary identity?
?
This argument shows a logical circle when trying to make non-existence an independent concept. In your own life, do you ever treat your sins or failures as if they have a reality that defines you? How does knowing that only existence (Allah) is truly real change how you view your shortcomings?

Razi's Rebuttal: Distinction Does Not Require Two Identities
Razi refutes the second argument by denying that distinction implies two identities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's rebuttal to the second argument: that distinction does not always imply two positive identities, as shown by the example of identity and non-identity.

Core Idea
Distinction can exist without both things being positive identities; non-identity is distinct from identity but is not an identity.

Razi responds to the previous argument by denying its key premise. He argues that distinction does not necessarily require both items to have a positive identity. His example of identity (huwiyya) and non-identity (lāhuwiyya) shows that two things can be distinct without non-identity being an identity. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise Razi denies
The second argument assumed that distinction requires two identities. Razi states this is not granted ('ghayru muslim'). He will provide a counterexample.
Notice the logical move: denial of the major premise.
2
Understand the example of identity and non-identity
Razi points out that identity (huwiyya) and non-identity (lāhuwiyya) are distinct, yet non-identity is not an identity. Thus, distinction does not require both to be identities.
This directly refutes the premise.
3
See how this avoids the vicious circle
If non-identity were given an identity, it would become a division of identity itself, not a separate thing. Similarly, the removal of non-existence does not create a separate identity, so the vicious circle does not follow.
The rebuttal preserves the coherence of distinguishing non-existence without positive identity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيها : لو سلمنا إمكان تصور العدم لكان قولنا النفى والإثبات لا يجتمعان يستدعى امتياز العدم الوجود ، وامتياز العدم عن الوجود يستدعى أن يكون لمسمى العدم هوية متميزة عن الوجود لكن ذلك محال ، لان كل هوية يشير العقل إليها و العقل يمكنه رفعها ، وإلا لم يكن له مقابل ، وكان يلزم أن لا يكون للعدم مقابل وكان يلزم نفى الوجود وهو باطل، فثبت أن ارتفاع الهوية المسماة بالعدم معقول ، لكر ارتفاع تلك الهوية ارتفاع خاص فيكون دا - لا النحت العدم المطلق ، فيكون قسم العدم قسما منه هذا خلف . أقول : الحكم بأن الامتياز يستدعى أن يكون للمتمايزين هويتان غير مسلم ، فإن الهوية واللاهوية ممتازان وليس اللاهوية هوية، ولو فرضنا لها هوية كانت بذلك الاعتبار داخلة في قسم الهوية وباعتبار ما فرض له هذا الاعتبار قسما للهوية ، وكذلك القول في رفع العدم ولا يلزم المخلف .
And the second: if we grant the possibility of conceiving non-existence, then our statement 'negation and affirmation do not combine' would require the distinction of non-existence from existence, and the distinction of non-existence from existence requires that the named non-existence have an identity (huwiyya) distinct from existence, but that is impossible, because every identity that the intellect points to, the intellect can remove it, otherwise it would have no opposite, and it would necessitate that non-existence have no opposite and necessitate the negation of existence, which is false. So it is established that the removal of the identity called non-existence is conceivable, because the removal of that identity is a specific removal, so it becomes the reality of absolute non-existence, so a division of non-existence becomes a part of it – that is a vicious circle (khalf). I say: the judgment that distinction requires that the two distinct things have two identities is not granted, for identity (huwiyya) and non-identity (lāhuwiyya) are distinct, and non-identity is not an identity; and if we assume it has an identity, it would by that consideration be included under the division of identity, and by the consideration of what is assumed for this consideration, it would be a partition of identity. Likewise for the removal of non-existence, and the vicious circle does not follow.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument (thāniyahā): even if we grant the conceivability of non-existence, the statement 'negation and affirmation do not combine' requires distinguishing non-existence from existence. This distinction presupposes that non-existence has an identity (huwiyya) distinct from existence. However, every identity can be removed by the intellect; otherwise it would have no opposite, leading to the negation of existence. Thus, the removal of that identity is conceivable, meaning that the removal itself becomes a specific removal that is absolute non-existence, causing a part of non-existence to be equal to the whole – a vicious circle (khalf). Razi refutes this by denying that distinction necessarily implies two distinct identities: identity and non-identity are distinct, but non-identity is not an identity. If one posits an identity for non-identity, it becomes a division of identity itself, not a separate thing. Thus, the regress is avoided.

Correction: Razi shows that identity and non-identity are distinct, yet non-identity has no positive identity; thus distinction does not require two positive identities.

Six Opinions on the Locus of the Soul
Razi enumerates six opinions on the locus of life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six different theological and philosophical opinions regarding the locus of life or soul in the human body as enumerated by Imam Razi.

Core Idea
Six schools, one question: where does life reside?

This passage from Imam Razi's Muhassal presents a fascinating survey of opinions on the nature and location of the soul or life. Before diving into his own argument, Razi catalogs six distinct views held by theologians, philosophers, and physicians. Understanding this landscape is essential for grasping the subsequent discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify each opinion
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. List the six opinions as they appear. Notice the key figures or schools associated with each.
2
Note the proponents
For each opinion, note the individual or group attributed to it: Ibn al-Rawandi, some theologians, al-Nazzam, Galen, etc. This shows the diversity of thinkers engaged.
3
Consider the underlying question
Reflect on why there are so many different theories. What is the central problem they are all trying to solve? The common thread is explaining the locus of life in the body.
This prepares you for Razi's own position.
4
Interpret the closing remark
The passage ends: 'The answer to it is what he mentioned.' What might Razi be implying? Consider that he may have already given his preferred view earlier in the text.
This remark signals that Razi does not leave the matter unresolved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السادس : فالقول بالجزء الذي لا يتجزأ في القلب مذهب ابن الراوندى والقول بالهيكل المحسوس مذهب بعض المتكلمين، ومذهب محققيهم أن تلك الهوية بأجزاء تتعلق بها الحياة ، والقول بالأجسام اللطيفة السارية فى البدن مذهب النظام من المعتزلة ، والقول بالمزاج مذهب جالينوس وبعض الأطباء ، والقول بالنفس الناطقة مذهب جمع من المتكلمين وجمهور الحكماء ، والجواب عنه ما ذكره .
As for the sixth: the statement about the indivisible part in the heart is the school of Ibn al-Rawandi; the statement about the perceptible structure is the school of some theologians; and the school of their verifiers is that those entity has parts by which life is attached; the statement about the subtle bodies flowing in the body is the school of al-Nazzam from the Mu'tazila; the statement about the temperament is the school of Galen and some physicians; the statement about the rational soul is the school of a group of theologians and the majority of philosophers. The answer to it is what he mentioned.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of six different opinions regarding the nature or locus of life (or the soul) in the human body. It appears as the sixth point in a larger discussion, likely about the human being or life. Razi lists the following schools: (1) The indivisible part in the heart: attributed to Ibn al-Rawandi. (2) The perceptible structure (hayaqil): some theologians. (3) The verifiers among theologians hold that the entity (al-hawiyya) has parts to which life is attached. (4) Subtle bodies flowing through the body: al-Nazzam of the Mu'tazila. (5) Temperament (mizaj): Galen and some physicians. (6) The rational soul: a group of theologians and most philosophers. The passage ends by noting that the answer to this diversity is what he (the author) has mentioned earlier, though the text does not reproduce that answer here.

Al-Razi's Refutation of Ibn Sina on the Sufficiency of Premises
Al-Razi refutes Ibn Sina's requirement of attentive subsumption.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's argument that any extra condition beyond the two premises, such as attentive subsumption, either leads to infinite regress or is logically incoherent.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina's extra condition collapses into infinite regress or emptiness.

Al-Razi engages in a high-level logical debate with Ibn Sina. Ibn Sina claimed that simply having two premises in mind is not enough for the conclusion; one also needs to be attentive to how the particular premise fits under the universal. Al-Razi dismantles this requirement with a simple dilemma, showing it leads to an infinite chain of premises or is no condition at all. He then addresses a potential counterargument involving recollection.

Guided Path
1
Understand Ibn Sina's claim
Ibn Sina argued that merely knowing two premises does not automatically yield the conclusion; one must also be actively aware of the subsumption relationship. Example: mule and sterility.
The mule example illustrates a case where one might fail to apply the universal.
2
Enter Al-Razi's dilemma
Al-Razi asks: Is the subsumption cognition distinct from the two premises or not? If distinct, it becomes a third premise, leading to infinite regress. If not distinct, it cannot be a condition because conditions must be distinct from what they condition.
This is a classic argument against adding extra conditions in logic.
3
Apply the dilemma to the mule example
The mule example only shows a failure to apply the universal, not a need for an additional cognition. The subsumption is already implicit in the knowledge of the two premises.
The example does not support Ibn Sina's claim.
4
Analyze the recollection counterargument
Ibn Sina might compare the needed attentiveness to recollection. Al-Razi responds that recollection without intent (from God) does not generate knowledge, while intentional reflection (activity of the servant) does. Thus, the analogy fails.
This draws on Mu'tazilite theory of generation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة ذكر ابن سينا أن حضور المقدمتين في الذهن لا يكفى الحصول النتيجة فإن الإنسان قد يعلم أن هذا الحيوان بغلة وأن كل بغلة عاقر ومع هذين العلمين ربما رأى بغلة منتفخة البطن فظن أنها غير عاقر ، بل لا بد مع حضور المقدمتين من التفطن لكيفية إدراج المقدمة الجزئية تحت الكلية وهذا ضعيف ، لأن اندراج إحدى المقدمتين تحت الأخرى إما أن يكون معلوما مغايرا لتلك المقدمتين وإما أن لا يكون ، فإن كان مغايرا كان ذلك مقدمة أخرى لابد منها فى الإنتاج، ويكون الكلام في كيفية التئامها مع الاولين كالكلام فى كيفية التئام الأولين ، ويفضى ذلك إلى اعتبار مالا نهاية له من المقدمات ، وإن لم يكن ذلك معلوما مغايرا للمقدمتين استحال أن يكون شرطا في الإنتاج؛ لأن الشرط مغاير للمشروط ، وههنا لا مغايرة فلا يكون شرطا ، وأما حديث البغلة فكذلك إنما يكون إذا كان الحاضر في الذهن إحدى المقدمتين في التذكر أيضا وهو أن يقولوا بتولد التذكر كما قالوا فى النظر بعينه وإنما أمكن ذلك لهم لأن أبا هاشم من المعتزلة قال بأن التذكر السائح للذهن من غير قصد لا يولد العلم التابع له ؛ لأن ذلك إنما يكون من فعل الله تعالى، والذي يفعله العبد بقصده واختياره فهو مولد لأن ذلك العلم إنما يحصل للعبد بسبب ما هو من فعله
Issue mentioned by Ibn Sina: The presence of two premises in the mind is not sufficient for obtaining the conclusion. For a person may know that this animal is a mule and that every mule is sterile, and with these two pieces of knowledge, he may see a mule with a swollen belly and think it is not sterile. Rather, along with the presence of the two premises, there must be attentiveness to how the particular premise is subsumed under the universal. And this is weak, because the subsumption of one premise under the other either is a cognition distinct from those two premises or not. If it is distinct, then it is another premise necessary for production, and the talk about how it fits with the first two is like the talk about how the first two fit together, and this leads to an infinite regress of premises. And if it is not distinct from the two premises, it is impossible for it to be a condition for production, because a condition is distinct from the conditioned, and here there is no distinctness, so it cannot be a condition. As for the story of the mule, that only happens when what is present in the mind is one of the two premises. Also regarding recollection, they say that generation occurs in recollection just as they said in reflection, and that is possible for them because Abu Hashim among the Mu'tazila said that recollection that flows through the mind without intent does not generate the knowledge that follows it; because that is only from God's act. And what the servant does by his intent and choice is generated, because that knowledge is obtained by the servant due to what is from his own action.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage refutes Ibn Sina's claim that the mere presence of two premises in the mind does not suffice for the conclusion; one also needs awareness of how the particular premise is subsumed under the universal. The author argues this is weak because the subsumption is either a distinct cognition or not. If distinct, it becomes an additional premise, leading to infinite regress. If not distinct, it cannot be a condition since a condition must be distinct from what it conditions. The mule example only shows a case where one premise is absent. The discussion then turns to recollection, where the Mu'tazila (following Abu Hashim) say recollection without intent does not generate knowledge (it is from God), but intentional reflection generates it. This explains why the analogy between reflection and recollection fails.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Sina's requirement of attentive subsumption is necessary and unproblematic.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that it either leads to infinite regress or is not a genuine condition, so it cannot be required.

Checkpoint

A) Because it is always present.
B) Because it leads to infinite regress of premises.
C) Because the mind subsumes automatically.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's dilemma effectively refutes Ibn Sina's extra condition by showing it leads to infinite regress or is logically incoherent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Mu'tazilite distinction between intentional and unintentional recollection raises the question: Are you striving intentionally to generate spiritual insight through your own effort, or are you open to the knowledge that God grants without your planning? Which approach does the Quran and Sunnah encourage?
?
If Al-Razi's argument shows that an extra condition is unnecessary, then when you have clear premises from revelation and reason, what prevents you from submitting to the conclusion? Is it a lack of attentiveness or a lack of will?

Razi's refutation of Ibn Sina on subsumption
Razi refutes Ibn Sina on subsumption awareness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's refutation of Ibn Sina's claim that awareness of subsumption is necessary for deduction, including the dilemma that leads to infinite regress or contradicts the condition of distinctness.

Core Idea
Awareness of subsumption either creates regress or is redundant.

This passage presents Imam Razi's logical critique of Ibn Sina's condition for deduction. Razi argues that requiring awareness of how premises combine leads to a destructive dilemma: either an infinite regress or an invalid condition. Understanding this refutation sharpens one's grasp of the foundations of deductive reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice how Razi structures his refutation: first stating Ibn Sina's claim, then presenting a counterexample, then a dilemma, then an analysis of the counterexample.
Pay attention to the logical connectors and conditional phrasing.
2
Identify Ibn Sina's claim and Razi's initial counterexample
Ibn Sina says presence of both premises is not enough; awareness of subsumption (kayfiyyat al-indiraj) is needed. Razi gives the mule example to show that doubt can arise even when both premises are known. Ask yourself: does this example really show insufficiency?
The mule example is meant to illustrate the need for awareness, but Razi later reinterprets it.
3
Analyze the dilemma: distinct or not distinct?
Razi presents two possibilities: the awareness of subsumption is either distinct from the two premises or not. If distinct, it becomes a third premise, and the same issue recurs, leading to infinite regress. If not distinct, it cannot be a condition because condition must be distinct from conditioned.
This is the core of the refutation. Understand why each horn is problematic.
4
Reinterpret the mule example
Razi argues that the mule example only works if one of the premises is absent from the mind. When both premises are present together, doubt about the conclusion is impossible. This dissolves the counterexample and supports Razi's position.
Consider what it means for a premise to be 'present in the mind'.
5
Summarize the argument in your own words
Try to restate Razi's refutation concisely. Include the dilemma and the handling of the counterexample. This will help internalize the logical structure.
You can use the key terms to ensure accuracy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة ذكر ابن سينا أن حضور المقدمتين في الذهن لا يكفي لحصول النتيجة. فإن الإنسان قد يعلم أن هذا الحيوان بغلة وأن كل بغلة عاقر ومع هذين العلمين ربما رأى بغلة منتفخة البطن فظن أنها غير عاقر، بل لا بد مع حضور المقدمتين من التفطن لكيفية اندراج المقدمة الجزئية تحت الكلية وهذا ضعيف، لأن اندراج إحدى المقدمتين تحت الأخرى إما أن يكون معلوما مغايرا لتلك المقدمتين وإما أن لا يكون، فإن كان مغايرا كان ذلك مقدمة أخرى لابد منها في الإنتاج، ويكون الكلام في كيفية التئامها مع الأولين كالكلام في كيفية التئام الأولين، ويفضي ذلك إلى اعتبار ما لا نهاية له من المقدمات، وإن لم يكن ذلك معلوما مغايرا للمقدمتين استحال أن يكون شرطا في الإنتاج؛ لأن الشرط مغاير للمشروط، وههنا لا مغايرة فلا يكون شرطا، وأما حديث البغلة فكذلك إنما يكون إذا كان الحاضر في الذهن إحدى المقدمتين فقط أما الصغرى أو الكبرى، أما عند اجتماعهما فلا يسلم أنه يمكن الشك في النتيجة.
Issue: Ibn Sina mentioned that the presence of the two premises in the mind is not sufficient for obtaining the conclusion. For a person may know that this animal is a mule and that every mule is barren, and with these two items of knowledge he perhaps sees a mule with a swollen belly and thinks it is not barren. Rather, along with the presence of the two premises, there must be awareness of how the particular premise is subsumed under the universal premise. But this is weak, because the subsumption of one premise under the other is either known as something distinct from those two premises or not. If it is distinct, then that is another premise necessary for the production, and the discussion of how it combines with the first two is like the discussion of how the first two combine, and that leads to an infinite regress of premises. And if it is not known as distinct from the two premises, it is impossible that it be a condition for the production, because the condition is distinct from the conditioned, and here there is no distinctness, so it is not a condition. As for the story of the mule, it only occurs when only one of the two premises is present in the mind, either the minor or the major; but when both are present together, it is not admitted that doubt about the conclusion is possible.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage refutes Ibn Sina's claim that the mere presence of both premises in the mind is insufficient for deduction; one must also be aware of the subsumption of the minor under the major. Imam Razi argues against this by analyzing the nature of subsumption. He presents a dilemma: the awareness of subsumption is either something distinct from the two premises or not. If distinct, it becomes a third premise, and combining it with the first two raises the same issue, leading to an infinite regress. If not distinct, it cannot be a condition because a condition must be distinct from the conditioned. Therefore, Ibn Sina's condition is invalid. Razi then addresses the counterexample of the mule: the doubt arises only when one of the premises is absent from the mind, not when both are present together. Thus, the objection fails.

Key Terms
المقدمتين
النتيجة
اندراج
الشرط
المشروط
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that awareness of subsumption is always necessary for deductive inference to occur.
Correction: Razi shows that requiring such awareness either leads to infinite regress or violates the condition of distinctness. In fact, when both premises are fully present in the mind, the conclusion follows without additional awareness.

Checkpoint
What is Imam Razi's main objection to Ibn Sina's claim that awareness of subsumption is necessary for deduction?
A) Awareness of subsumption is impossible to achieve.
B) It leads to an infinite regress of premises or contradicts the condition of distinctness.
C) The example of the mule proves that awareness is not needed.
D) Ibn Sina's argument contradicts the Qur'an.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi refutes Ibn Sina by showing that requiring awareness of subsumption either leads to infinite regress or is an invalid condition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you have clear evidence from the Qur'an and Sunnah, do you sometimes hesitate to act because you are looking for an extra 'logical connection' that you think is needed? How does Razi's argument expose this as either an infinite regress or a meaningless condition? What will you do this week when faced with a clear command from Allah?
?
Have you ever found yourself doubting a truth of faith because you felt you needed to 'understand' the mechanism (subsumption) before accepting it? Razi shows that requiring such extra awareness is invalid. In what area of your life—tawakkul, qadr, or worship—are you holding back due to this? What step will you take to stop demanding a third premise and submit fully?
?
The mule owner knew both premises but still doubted—Razi says he must have forgotten one. Think of a promise from Allah that you know intellectually but fail to trust emotionally. Which premise (e.g., Allah’s power, His mercy) are you allowing to slip from your mind? What concrete action will you take to keep both premises present and active in your heart today?

Razi's Correction: Defining Evidence and Indicator
Razi corrects the definition of evidence and clarifies entailment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's corrected definition of evidence (dalīl) and indicator (amāra), and how he clarifies the notion of entailment (luzūm) between correlates.

Core Idea
Proof yields certainty; indicator yields opinion; entailment can be reciprocal.

This passage is a crucial intervention by Imam Razi, correcting a previous definition of evidence. He distinguishes between proof, which produces certain knowledge, and indicator, which only yields opinion. He then refines the understanding of entailment, showing that two-sided entailment between correlates is essentially a two-way causation. This lesson will clarify these logical distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the passage carefully, noting the authoritative 'Aqūl' (I say). This is a direct correction from Imam Razi.
2
Identify the two key definitions
Find where Razi defines proof (dalīl) and indicator (amāra). Note the difference in what they yield: knowledge vs. opinion.
3
Examine the critique of entailment
Razi criticizes the earlier statement about entailment occurring from one side. Understand his point about two-sided entailment and correlates.
He argues that if entailment is mutual, the two sides are correlates.
4
Compare entailment in correlates vs cause-effect
Razi says entailment between correlates is like cause-effect, but two-sided. Reflect on how this deepens understanding of logical relations.
This is a nuanced point about the nature of relation (iḍāfa).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : الصواب أن يقال : الدليل هو الذى يلزم من المنظر فيه العلم بالمدلول ، فإن من المدلول مالا وجود له ويستدل عليه كنفى العلم الذى يستدل عليه بنفى الحياة ، وكذلك الدليل والأمارة هي الذي يلزم من النظر فيها الظن بالمدلول. وأما قوله « فاللزوم حاصل لا محالة من هذا الطرف وإن حصل من الطرف الآخر فهو كذا وكذا ، فلا شك في أن اللزوم إذا حصل من الطرفين كان الطرفان متلازمين ثم قوله بعد ذلك, أو بأحد الملازمين على الآخر ، دو عين ما قاله أولا لأنه قسيمه، والاختلاف بينهما ليس إلا بالأمثلة والتلازم بين المضافين فى الحقيقة ليس مغايرا لماذكر فى العملة والمعلول، لأن ذات كل واحد من المضافين علة اللإضافة المتعلقة بالآخر فهو نوع من دلالة العملة على المعلول ، إلا أنه واقع مرتين في الجانبين والباقى ظاهر
I say: The correct thing is to say: The proof (dalīl) is that from the consideration of which knowledge of the indicated (madlūl) necessarily follows. For among the indicated is that which has no existence and is inferred, like the negation of knowledge which is inferred from the negation of life. Likewise, the indicator (al-amāra) is that from consideration of which opinion (ẓann) of the indicated follows. As for his statement 'So the entailment necessarily occurs from this side, and if it occurs from the other side, then it is such and such'—there is no doubt that if entailment occurs from both sides, then the two sides are correlates (mutalāzimān). Then his statement afterwards 'or by one of the two correlates from the other' is exactly what he said first, because it is its correlate. The difference between them is only in examples. And the entailment between the correlatives (al-muḍāfayn) is in reality not different from what was mentioned concerning cause and effect, because the essence of each of the two correlatives is the cause of the relation (al-iḍāfa) pertaining to the other. So it is a type of indication of the cause for the effect, except that it occurs twice on both sides. And the rest is apparent.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is an authorial commentary (أقول) correcting the previous definition. The correct definition of proof (dalīl) is that which, when considered, necessarily yields knowledge of the indicated. For non-existent things inferred negatively (e.g., negation of life indicating negation of knowledge), it works similarly. The indicator (amāra) yields opinion (ẓann), not certainty. The author then criticizes the earlier statement about entailment occurring from one side only: if entailment occurs from both sides, they are correlates. The later statement about correlates is redundant because it repeats the same idea. The difference is merely in examples. The entailment between correlatives is essentially the same as cause-effect because each correlative's essence is the cause of its relation to the other; it is just a two-sided causation. This nuance refines the understanding of logical relations in evidence.

Key Terms

أمارة
لزوم
ظن
علم
متلازمان
مضافان
علة
معلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Both evidence and indicator produce certain knowledge.
Correction: Evidence (dalīl) yields certain knowledge, while indicator (amāra) yields only opinion (ẓann).

Checkpoint
According to the corrected definition, what does the indicator (amāra) produce?
A) Certain knowledge (ʿilm)
B) Opinion (ẓann)
C) Both knowledge and opinion
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
Razi's correction establishes that evidence yields certainty, indicators yield opinion, and entailment can be reciprocal between correlates, resembling cause-effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read or hear an Islamic argument, do you always check whether it gives you certainty (ʿilm) or just an opinion (ẓann)? If not, how often have you settled for opinion when you needed certainty—and what was the cost?
?
Think of a belief you hold strongly about your faith. Can you trace it back to a proof (dalīl) that yields certainty, or is it built on indicators (amārāt) that only yield opinion? Are you willing to test it today?
?
Razi shows that entailment can be two-sided: each thing causes the other's relation. In your own life, are there beliefs or habits that feed each other in a cycle? Which one would you need to break first to change the other—and what will you do about it this week?
