Classification of Proof: Specific and General
Razi classifies inferences based on specificity relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi classifies inferences based on the specificity relation between proof and indicated, distinguishing logical syllogism, induction, and juristic analogy.

Core Idea
Inference is ruled by specificity: general-to-specific, specific-to-general, or shared attribute.

Razi begins by establishing a fundamental classification of inference based on whether the proof (dalīl) and the indicated (madlūl) are in a relation of greater specificity. This logical distinction is crucial for understanding both philosophical and legal reasoning. The passage lays out three possibilities: inference from general to specific (syllogism), from specific to general (induction), and when neither is more specific (requiring a shared attribute, giving juristic analogy).

Guided Path
1
Read the classification passage
Read the Arabic text from 'مسألة' to 'مركب من القسمين الأولين' along with the translation. Notice the three distinct cases.
Razi uses 'أخص' (more specific) as the key criterion.
2
Identify the three inference types
List the three types: (1) general to specific - syllogism in logicians' terms, (2) specific to general - induction, (3) neither - requires a common description, giving juristic qiyas.
Remember that the third type combines elements of the first two.
3
Grasp the role of the common description
Understand why a common attribute (waṣf mushtarak) is necessary when neither is more specific: it allows transfer of judgment by identifying the criterion (manāṭ).
This is the foundation of analogical reasoning in Islamic jurisprudence.
4
Distinguish the two qiyas
Note that Razi distinguishes qiyas in logic (general to specific) from qiyas in fiqh (based on common description). This is a common point of confusion.
The same term 'qiyas' has different meanings in different disciplines.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : الدليل والمدلول إما أن يكون أحدهما أخص من الثانى أو لا ) إذا استدللنا بشيء على شيء فإما أن يكون أحدهما أخص من الثانى أو لا يكون ، والأول على قسمين ، لأنه إما أن يستدل بالعام على الخاص وهو القياس في عرف المنطقيين أو بالعكس وهو الاستقراء. وأما الثاني فلا يمكن الاستدلال بأحدهما على الآخر إلا إذا اندرجا تحت وصف مشترك بينهما ، فيستدل بثبوت الحكم في إحدى الصورتين على أن المناط هو المشترك ، ثم يستدل بذلك على ثبوته فى الصورة الأخرى وهو القياس في عرف الفقهاء . وهو في الحقيقة مركب من القسمين الأولين ، ثم القياس بالمعنى الأول على خمسة أقسام . أحدها : أن نحكم بلزوم شىء لشىء فيلزم من وجود الملزوم وجود اللازم ومن عدم اللازم عدم الملزوم تحقيقا للزوم، ولا يلزم من عدم الملزوم عدم اللازم ولا من وجود اللازم وجود الملزوم تصحيحا للعموم. وثانيها : التقسيم المنحصر إلى قسمين فإنه يلزم من دفع أيهما كان ثبوت الآخر ومن ثبوت أيهما كان ارتفاع الآخر . ثالثها : إذا حكمنا بثبوت الألف لكل ما ثبت له الباء أو بانتفاء الألف عن كل ما ثبت له الباء ثم رأينا الباء ثابتا لكل الجيم أو لبعضه حكمنا بثبوت الألف أو بانتفائه. ورابعها : إذا حكمنا بالألف ثابتا للباء ومسلوبا عن الجيم ، فإن كان وقت السلب والإيجاب واحدا كفى ذلك في مباينة الطرفين ، فأما إذا لم يعين الوقت لم ينتج إلا عند اعتبار الدوام في أحد الطرفين ، لأن دوام إحدى القضيتين يوجب الآخر كيف كان . وخامسها : إذا حصل وصفان في محل واحد فقد التقيا فيه أما فى الخارج عنه فربما يحصل ذلك الالتقاء وربما لا يحصل ، فلا جرم كان اللازم منه هو الحكم الجزئى، وتفاصيل هذه المناهج مذكور في كتبنا المنطقية.
Issue: The proof (dalīl) and the indicated (madlūl) are either such that one is more specific (akhaṣṣ) than the other or not. If we infer from something to something, then either one is more specific than the other or not. The first [case] is of two types: either one infers from the general to the specific – this is qiyās (syllogism) in the terminology of the logicians – or the reverse, which is istiqrāʾ (induction). As for the second [case where neither is more specific], it is not possible to infer one from the other except when they fall under a common description (waṣf mushtarak). Then the affirmation of the ruling in one of the two cases is used to argue that the criterion (al-manāṭ) is the common description, and then it is used to argue for its affirmation in the other case – this is qiyās in the terminology of the jurists (fuqahāʾ). In reality, it is composite of the first two types. Then qiyās in the first sense is of five types: First: we judge the necessary entailment (luzūm) of something for something, so the existence of the entailed (al-malzūm) necessitates the existence of the necessary (al-lāzim), and the non-existence of the necessary necessitates the non-existence of the entailed, to realize the entailment; but the non-existence of the entailed does not necessitate the non-existence of the necessary, and the existence of the necessary does not necessitate the existence of the entailed, to correct the universality. Second: the exclusive division into two parts – the repulsion of either one necessitates the affirmation of the other, and the affirmation of either one necessitates the removal of the other. Third: If we judge the affirmation of A for everything that has B, or the negation of A from everything that has B, then we see that B is affirmed for all or some of C, we judge the affirmation or negation of A. Fourth: If we judge that A is affirmed for B and negated for C, if the time of negation and affirmation is one, that suffices for the separation of the two sides; but if the time is not specified, it does not yield a result except when considering perpetuity (dawām) in one of the two sides, because the perpetuity of one of the two propositions necessitates the other, however it may be. Fifth: If two attributes occur in one locus, they have met in it; as for outside it, perhaps that meeting occurs and perhaps not, so inevitably what follows from it is the particular judgment. The details of these methods are mentioned in our logical works.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage classifies types of inference based on the logical relation of specificity between proof and indicated. If one is more specific, inference is either syllogism (general to specific) or induction (specific to general). If neither is more specific, inference requires a shared common attribute, leading to analogical reasoning (qiyās) as used by jurists, which combines the first two types. The passage then lists five types of qiyās in the logical sense: (1) necessary entailment (luzūm) with the condition that existence of the entailed requires existence of the necessary, non-existence of necessary entails non-existence of entailed, but not the reverse; (2) exclusive division into two contradictories; (3) if A holds for all B and B holds for all/some C, then A holds for C; (4) if A is affirmed for B and denied for C, with a defined time, it shows separation, otherwise permanence is needed; (5) when two attributes converge in a single subject, the resulting judgment is particular. This detailed logical framework is foundational for kalam argumentation, and the author refers to his logical works for further details.

Key Terms
دليل
مدلول
أخص
قياس
استقراء
مناط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that qiyas (analogy) in Islam is the same as the logical syllogism.
Correction: Razi clarifies that logical syllogism goes from general to specific, while juristic qiyas relies on a shared attribute and is a combination of induction and deduction.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what type of inference is used when the proof and the indicated are not more specific than each other?
A) Logical syllogism (qiyās of logicians)
B) Induction (istiqrāʾ)
C) Qiyās of jurists based on a common description
D) Direct entailment

Key Takeaway
Razi classifies inferences into three types: syllogism (general to specific), induction (specific to general), and analogy via common attribute (juristic qiyas).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you argue for a religious ruling, are you aware of whether you are using deduction, induction, or analogy? How does Razi's classification challenge you to be more precise in your reasoning?
?
Think of an analogy you recently used to convince someone of something. Did you examine whether the two cases truly share a common attribute (المناط)? What would it mean if they only appear similar but the underlying criterion is different?
?
Your intellectual habits likely favor one type of inference over others. Which do you rely on most, and how might that limit your understanding of complex issues? Will you now consciously seek to employ the other forms when appropriate?

Logic and Technical Terms

Five Types of Syllogism (Qiyas) in Logic
Razi enumerates five types of logical syllogism with their rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the five types of logical syllogism (qiyas) as outlined by Razi: entailment, exclusive division, categorical syllogism, temporal separation, and conjunction of attributes.

Core Idea
Logical syllogism has five defined patterns governing necessary relations, division, and inference from shared attributes.

Having distinguished logical syllogism from juristic analogy, Razi now details the five types of syllogism used in logical reasoning. Each type has specific rules for what follows from what, and these patterns are foundational for constructing proofs in kalam. This passage is a concise enumeration of those patterns, each illustrated with conditional relations.

Guided Path
1
Read the five types passage
Read from 'ثم القياس بالمعنى الأول' to the end of the passage, along with translation. Identify each of the five types listed.
The Arabic uses 'أحدها', 'ثانيها', etc.
2
Understand the first type: entailment (luzūm)
Note the conditional: existence of entailed implies existence of necessary; non-existence of necessary implies non-existence of entailed. The reverse does not hold.
This is the standard definition of necessary entailment.
3
Analyze the second type: exclusive division
In a dichotomy, denying one affirms the other; affirming one removes the other.
This is logical exclusivity between two mutually exclusive parts.
4
Examine the third and fourth types
Third: if A holds for all B and B holds for all/some C, then A holds for C. Fourth: affirmation of A for B and negation for C with same time shows separation; otherwise consider perpetuity.
These are versions of categorical syllogism and separation.
5
Understand the fifth type: conjunction of attributes
When two attributes meet in one subject, the consequent judgment is particular.
This covers cases where shared attributes yield a specific ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : الدليل والمدلول إما أن يكون أحدهما أخص من الثانى أو لا ) إذا استدللنا بشيء على شيء فإما أن يكون أحدهما أخص من الثانى أو لا يكون ، والأول على قسمين ، لأنه إما أن يستدل بالعام على الخاص وهو القياس في عرف المنطقيين أو بالعكس وهو الاستقراء. وأما الثاني فلا يمكن الاستدلال بأحدهما على الآخر إلا إذا اندرجا تحت وصف مشترك بينهما ، فيستدل بثبوت الحكم في إحدى الصورتين على أن المناط هو المشترك ، ثم يستدل بذلك على ثبوته فى الصورة الأخرى وهو القياس في عرف الفقهاء . وهو في الحقيقة مركب من القسمين الأولين ، ثم القياس بالمعنى الأول على خمسة أقسام . أحدها : أن نحكم بلزوم شىء لشىء فيلزم من وجود الملزوم وجود اللازم ومن عدم اللازم عدم الملزوم تحقيقا للزوم، ولا يلزم من عدم الملزوم عدم اللازم ولا من وجود اللازم وجود الملزوم تصحيحا للعموم. وثانيها : التقسيم المنحصر إلى قسمين فإنه يلزم من دفع أيهما كان ثبوت الآخر ومن ثبوت أيهما كان ارتفاع الآخر . ثالثها : إذا حكمنا بثبوت الألف لكل ما ثبت له الباء أو بانتفاء الألف عن كل ما ثبت له الباء ثم رأينا الباء ثابتا لكل الجيم أو لبعضه حكمنا بثبوت الألف أو بانتفائه. ورابعها : إذا حكمنا بالألف ثابتا للباء ومسلوبا عن الجيم ، فإن كان وقت السلب والإيجاب واحدا كفى ذلك في مباينة الطرفين ، فأما إذا لم يعين الوقت لم ينتج إلا عند اعتبار الدوام في أحد الطرفين ، لأن دوام إحدى القضيتين يوجب الآخر كيف كان . وخامسها : إذا حصل وصفان في محل واحد فقد التقيا فيه أما فى الخارج عنه فربما يحصل ذلك الالتقاء وربما لا يحصل ، فلا جرم كان اللازم منه هو الحكم الجزئى، وتفاصيل هذه المناهج مذكور في كتبنا المنطقية.
Issue: The proof (dalīl) and the indicated (madlūl) are either such that one is more specific (akhaṣṣ) than the other or not. If we infer from something to something, then either one is more specific than the other or not. The first [case] is of two types: either one infers from the general to the specific – this is qiyās (syllogism) in the terminology of the logicians – or the reverse, which is istiqrāʾ (induction). As for the second [case where neither is more specific], it is not possible to infer one from the other except when they fall under a common description (waṣf mushtarak). Then the affirmation of the ruling in one of the two cases is used to argue that the criterion (al-manāṭ) is the common description, and then it is used to argue for its affirmation in the other case – this is qiyās in the terminology of the jurists (fuqahāʾ). In reality, it is composite of the first two types. Then qiyās in the first sense is of five types: First: we judge the necessary entailment (luzūm) of something for something, so the existence of the entailed (al-malzūm) necessitates the existence of the necessary (al-lāzim), and the non-existence of the necessary necessitates the non-existence of the entailed, to realize the entailment; but the non-existence of the entailed does not necessitate the non-existence of the necessary, and the existence of the necessary does not necessitate the existence of the entailed, to correct the universality. Second: the exclusive division into two parts – the repulsion of either one necessitates the affirmation of the other, and the affirmation of either one necessitates the removal of the other. Third: If we judge the affirmation of A for everything that has B, or the negation of A from everything that has B, then we see that B is affirmed for all or some of C, we judge the affirmation or negation of A. Fourth: If we judge that A is affirmed for B and negated for C, if the time of negation and affirmation is one, that suffices for the separation of the two sides; but if the time is not specified, it does not yield a result except when considering perpetuity (dawām) in one of the two sides, because the perpetuity of one of the two propositions necessitates the other, however it may be. Fifth: If two attributes occur in one locus, they have met in it; as for outside it, perhaps that meeting occurs and perhaps not, so inevitably what follows from it is the particular judgment. The details of these methods are mentioned in our logical works.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage classifies types of inference based on the logical relation of specificity between proof and indicated. If one is more specific, inference is either syllogism (general to specific) or induction (specific to general). If neither is more specific, inference requires a shared common attribute, leading to analogical reasoning (qiyās) as used by jurists, which combines the first two types. The passage then lists five types of qiyās in the logical sense: (1) necessary entailment (luzūm) with the condition that existence of the entailed requires existence of the necessary, non-existence of necessary entails non-existence of entailed, but not the reverse; (2) exclusive division into two contradictories; (3) if A holds for all B and B holds for all/some C, then A holds for C; (4) if A is affirmed for B and denied for C, with a defined time, it shows separation, otherwise permanence is needed; (5) when two attributes converge in a single subject, the resulting judgment is particular. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that entailment (luzūm) is symmetric (if A entails B, then B entails A).
Correction: Razi specifies that the reverse does not hold: from existence of the necessary, the existence of the entailed does not follow, nor from non-existence of entailed does non-existence of necessary follow.

Checkpoint
According to the first type of qiyas, what necessarily follows from the non-existence of the necessary (lāzim)?
A) The non-existence of the entailed (malzūm)
B) The existence of the entailed
C) Nothing can be concluded
D) The existence of the entailed is impossible

Key Takeaway
Razi defines five patterns of logical syllogism: entailment, exclusive division, categorical syllogism, temporal separation, and conjunction of attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you assume that something is true because its consequence is true, are you aware that you might be committing the logical fallacy of affirming the consequent? How often do you check the direction of entailment in your own reasoning?
?
In your daily judgments, do you ever rely on mutually exclusive categories without confirming they are truly exhaustive? Think of a time when you assumed 'either this or that' but missed a third option. How can you train yourself to question such divisions?
?
The fourth type of syllogism shows that time context matters for valid inference. When you make a judgment about someone's character or a situation, do you consider the time frame of the evidence? Are you mixing different times? How can you be more precise about temporality in your conclusions?

Classification of Inference: Qiyas, Istiqra', and Qiyas al-Fuqaha'
Al-Razi classifies inference into three types based on generality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi classifies types of inference based on the relative generality of the evidence and the thing evidenced, distinguishing between syllogism (qiyas), induction (istiqra'), and juristic analogy (qiyas al-fuqaha').

Core Idea
Inference is classified by the relationship of specificity between evidence and conclusion.

Al-Razi begins this section by framing the investigation in terms of a fundamental question: when we use one thing as evidence for another, are they of equal scope, or is one more specific? The answer gives rise to three distinct forms of inference, each with its own logical structure. This passage establishes the conceptual map that underlies much of later Islamic logic and legal theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the initial dichotomy
Read the first sentence carefully: 'When we infer something from something, then either one of them is more specific than the other or not.' This is the branching point.
Notice that this is a logical division into two mutually exclusive categories.
2
Identify the two cases when one is more specific
If one is more specific, there are two subcases: inferring general from specific (syllogism) and specific from general (induction). Identify each and note their names in Arabic.
Syllogism is called qiyas by logicians; induction is istiqra'.
3
Understand the case of equal generality
When both are equal in generality, inference requires a common description. This third type is qiyas al-fuqaha' (juristic analogy). Note how it combines elements of the first two.
This type is fundamental in Islamic jurisprudence.
4
Reflect on the overall classification
Take a moment to see how three types cover all possibilities: specific-general, general-specific, and equal-with-common-description. This is a complete classification.
Al-Razi states that juristic analogy is actually composed of the first two types.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : الدليل والمدلول إما أن يكون أحدهما أخص من الثانى أو لا إذا استدللنا بشيء على شيء فإما أن يكون أحدهما أخص من الثانى أو لا يكون ، والأول على قسمين ، لأنه إما أن يستدل بالعام على الخاص وهو القياس في عرف المنطقيين أو بالعكس وهو الاستقراء. وأما الثاني فلا يمكن الاستدلال بأحدهما على الآخر إلا إذا اندرجا تحت وصف مشترك بينهما ، فيستدل بثبوت الحكم في إحدى الصورتين على أن المناط هو المشترك ، ثم يستدل بذلك على ثبوته في الصورة الأخرى وهو القياس في عرف الفقهاء . وهو في الحقيقة مركب من القسمين الأولين ، ثم القياس بالمعنى الأول على خمسة أقسام . أحدها : أن نحكم بلزوم شيء لشيء فيلزم من وجود الملزوم وجود اللازم ومن عدم اللازم عدم الملزوم تحقيقا للزوم، ولا يلزم من عدم الملزوم عدم اللازم ولا من وجود اللازم وجود الملزوم تصحيحا للعموم. وثانيها : التقسيم المنحصر إلى قسمين فإنه يلزم من دفع أيهما كان ثبوت الآخر ومن ثبوت أيهما كان ارتفاع الآخر . وثالثها : إذا حكمنا بثبوت الألف لكل ما ثبت له الباء أو بانتفاء الألف عن كل ما ثبت له الباء ثم رأينا الباء ثابتا لكل الجيم أو لبعضه حكمنا بثبوت الألف ورابعها : إذا حكمنا بالألف ثابتا للباء ومسلوبا عن الجيم ، فإن كان وقت السلب والإيجاب واحدا كفى ذلك في مباينة الطرفين ، فأما إذا لم يعين الوقت لم ينتج إلا عند اعتبار الدوام في أحد الطرفين ، لأن دوام إحدى القضيتين يوجب الآخر كيف كان . وخامسها : إذا حصل وصفان في محل واحد فقد التقيا فيه أما في الخارج عنه فربما يحصل ذلك الالتقاء وربما لا يحصل ، فلا جرم كان اللازم منه هو الحكم الجزئي، وتفاصيل هذه المناهج مذكور في كتبنا المنطقية.
Question: The evidence and the thing evidenced are either one is more specific than the other or not. When we infer something from something, then either one of them is more specific than the other or not. And the first is of two types: because either one infers the general from the specific, which is qiyas in the terminology of logicians, or the opposite, which is istiqra'. And as for the second, it is not possible to infer one from the other unless they fall under a common description between them, so one infers from the establishment of the judgment in one of the two cases that the criterion is the common thing, then one infers by that its establishment in the other case, and that is qiyas in the terminology of jurists. And it is in reality composed of the first two types. Then qiyas in the first sense is of five divisions. First: that we judge a thing a necessary concomitant of a thing, so from the existence of the necessary concomitant existence of the necessary, and from the non-existence of the necessary the non-existence of the necessary concomitant, to achieve the necessary connection; and it does not follow from the non-existence of the necessary concomitant the non-existence of the necessary, nor from the existence of the necessary the existence of the necessary concomitant, to correct the generality. Second: exclusive division into two, for from the negation of either one follows the affirmation of the other, and from the affirmation of either one follows the negation of the other. Third: if we judge the affirmation of A for everything to which B is affirmed, or the negation of A from everything to which B is affirmed, then we see that B is affirmed for all of J or for some of it, we judge the affirmation of A. Fourth: if we judge A affirmed for B and denied for J, then if the time of the negation and affirmation is one, that suffices for the separation of the two extremes; but if the time is not specified, it does not produce a result except when considering permanence in one of the two extremes, because the persistence of one of the two propositions necessitates the other however it may be. Fifth: if two attributes occur in one locus, then they have met in it; as for outside it, perhaps that meeting occurs and perhaps not; necessarily, the necessary consequence is the particular judgment. And the details of these methods are mentioned in our logical books.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage defines the types of inference (istidlal) based on the relationship between the evidence (dalil) and the thing evidenced (madlul). Al-Razi first considers whether one is more specific than the other. If one is more specific, there are two subcases: (1) inferring the general from the specific, which is qiyas in the logician's sense (syllogism), and (2) inferring the specific from the general, which is istiqra' (induction). If they are equal in generality, inference is only possible if they share a common description. In that case, one infers from the presence of a judgment in one instance that the common attribute is the reason ('illa), then applies that to another instance. This is qiyas in the jurist's sense (analogical deduction). Al-Razi notes that the third type is actually a combination of the first two. He then enumerates five subdivisions of syllogistic qiyas: (1) implication of necessary concomitance, (2) exclusive disjunction, (3) inference via a common predicate, (4) simultaneous affirmation and negation, (5) two attributes in one subject. Each is briefly illustrated. The passage ends by referring to his logical works for fuller treatment.

استقراء
قياس الفقهاء

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All inference is either deductive or inductive; there is no third type.
Correction: Al-Razi introduces a third type, juristic analogy, which operates when evidence and conclusion have equal generality and requires a shared attribute.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi identifies three types of inference: deduction (general from specific), induction (specific from general), and analogy (when both are equal in generality).

?

In your own life, have you ever relied solely on one type of reasoning (e.g., only analogy without considering general principles)? How does this affect the soundness of your conclusions? ?

The Five Subdivisions of Syllogistic Qiyas
Al-Razi enumerates five subdivisions of syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five distinct logical forms that syllogistic qiyas can take according to Al-Razi, including implication, exclusive division, common predicate, simultaneous affirmation/negation, and two attributes in one subject.

Core Idea
Syllogistic reasoning has five canonical forms, each with its own inferential rules.

Having introduced the broad classification, Al-Razi now turns to the first type—syllogistic qiyas—and breaks it down into five subdivisions. These are the core logical structures that underpin deductive argumentation in his system. Each subdivision is defined by the relationship between the terms and the conditions for valid inference.

Guided Path
1
Read the enumeration of five divisions
Read the Arabic text from 'Then qiyas in the first sense is of five divisions' through the end. Notice each division is introduced with 'one of them is...', 'second', etc.
Al-Razi uses ordinal numbers to list the divisions.
2
Understand the first division: necessary concomitance
This division deals with a necessary relationship between two things (malzum and lazim). Note the conditions: from existence of malzum we infer existence of lazim, and from nonexistence of lazim we infer nonexistence of malzum, but not the converse.
This is a form of conditional reasoning.
3
Understand the second division: exclusive division
This is a logical disjunction where two options are mutually exclusive and exhaustive. Affirming one denies the other, and denying one affirms the other.
This is the principle of excluded middle applied to two alternatives.
4
Understand the remaining three divisions
For divisions three, four, and five, read carefully: the third uses a common predicate, the fourth deals with time conditions, and the fifth involves two attributes in one subject. Note the specific conditions for each.
These are more complex forms.
5
Reflect on the completeness of the taxonomy
Al-Razi ends by saying details are in his logical works, implying these five are a foundational classification. Consider how each form serves a different logical purpose.
This taxonomy is influential in later Islamic logic.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Three Types of Inference According to al-Razi
Introduction to the three divisions of inference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three types of inference that al-Razi enumerates in his preface to the detailed proofs.

Core Idea
Rational inference takes three forms: logical syllogism, induction, and analogical reasoning.

In a brief yet eloquent passage, al-Razi introduces the division of inferences that forms the backbone of his argumentation. The gloss begins by highlighting his conciseness and then lists the three categories before deferring the first. This lesson sets the stage for understanding his method.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to see how al-Razi introduces the three types. Notice his praise for his own method.
The passage starts with 'I say: He intends...' indicating a gloss on al-Razi's text.
2
Identify the three types
List the three types: qiyas of the logicians, istiqra', and qiyas of the jurists (tamthil). Note that the first is deferred.
This classification is key to the entire discussion.
3
Reflect on the purpose
Ask yourself why al-Razi would present such a division at the start. What does it tell us about his approach to argumentation?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : يريد إيراد جمع الحجج مفصلة بقول موجز وهو في الغاية الحسن والبلاغة ، وذلك أنه قسم الاستدلالات إلى ثلاثة أقسام قياس المنطقيين والاستقراء وقياس الفقهاء المسمى عند المنطقيين بالتمثيل وأخر القول في القسم الأول، أما الاستقراء فهو الحكم على كل ما ثبت جزئياته ، فإن كانت الجزئيات محصورة سمي بالاستقراء التام والقياس المقسم كقولنا العدد إما زوج وإما فرد ، وكل زوج يعد بالواحد وكل فرد يعد بالواحد وكل عدد يعد بالواحد وهذا يقيني ، وإن لم تكن الجزئيات محصورة فذلك الحكم يكون ظنيا لاحتمال أن يكون جزئي غير ما ذكر بخلاف ما ذكر والمثال المشهور فيه الحكم بأن كل حيوان يحرك فكه الأسفل عند المضغ لكون الناس وجميع البهائم والسباع كذلك وذلك الحكم غير يقيني ربما يقع فيه مخالفة في جزئي غير هذه الجزئيات كالتمساح ، فإنه يحرك الفك الأعلى عند المضغ وأما قياس الفقهاء فظني أيضا لأن ثبوت الحكم في إحدى الصورتين لا يدل على أن علة ذلك الثبوت هو الأمر المشترك ، ولو ثبت أن المشترك علة لذلك الثبوت فمن الجائز أن يكون علة خاصة بتلك الصورة ، أعني يكون خصوصية تلك الصورة شرطا في عليتها أما إن ثبت أن عليته للحكم عام حيث كان هذا القسم إلى القسم الأول أعني الاستدلال بالكلي على جزئياته ، وصار ذكر الصورة بكون الحكم فيه لها ثابتا حشوا لا تأثير له أصلا، وإنما يختص هذا بالانتهاء لأنهم يكتفون بحصول الظن ولا يستعمله جميعهم أيضا . رجع . أما قوله : « هو بالحقيقة مركب من الأولين ، فلأنه يستدل فيه من جزئي على كلي كاف الاستقراء إلا أن الاستقراء لا يقتضى على جزئي واحد ثم يستدل من ذلك الكلي على الجزئي الآخر ، وذلك أيضا ليس يقينيا فهي مركب ما يشبه الأولين وليس مهما .
I say: He intends to present a collection of proofs in detail with a concise statement, and it is in the utmost beauty and eloquence. That is, he divided inferences into three types: qiyas of the logicians, istiqra', and qiyas of the jurists, called tamthil by the logicians. He deferred the discussion of the first type. As for istiqra', it is judging about everything whose particulars are established. If the particulars are limited, it is called complete induction and the syllogism of division, as when we say: number is either even or odd, and every even is counted by one, and every odd is counted by one, and every number is counted by one — this is certain. But if the particulars are not limited, then that judgment is probable, due to the possibility that there is a particular other than what was mentioned, contrary to what was mentioned. The famous example is the judgment that every animal moves its lower jaw when chewing, because humans and all beasts and predators do so. That judgment is not certain; there may occur a discrepancy in a particular other than these particulars, like the crocodile, for it moves its upper jaw when chewing. As for qiyas of the jurists, it is also probable because the establishment of the judgment in one of the two cases does not indicate that the cause of that establishment is the common attribute; and even if it is established that the common attribute is the cause of that establishment, it is possible that it is a cause specific to that case, i.e., the specificity of that case is a condition for its causality. But if it is established that its causality for the judgment is general, then this type falls under the first type, i.e., inference from the universal to its particulars, and the mention of the case as having the judgment becomes redundant, having no effect at all. This is only specific to the terminological usage because they are content with obtaining probability, and not all of them use it either. Return. As for his statement: 'It is in reality composed of the first two,' it is because in it one infers from a particular to a universal, like the gist of istiqra', except that istiqra' does not require a single particular, then one infers from that universal to the other particular, and that also is not certain. So it is a composite resembling the first two, but it is not them.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This is a marginal gloss explaining Al-Razi's concise statement. It begins by noting that Al-Razi divides inferences into three types: (1) qiyas of the logicians, (2) istiqra' (induction), and (3) qiyas of the jurists (called tamthil by logicians). The gloss then elaborates on istiqra': if the enumeration of particulars is exhaustive (complete induction), it yields certainty, as in the division of numbers into even and odd. If not exhaustive, it yields probability, as in the generalization about animals moving their lower jaw when chewing, which is refuted by the crocodile moving its upper jaw. Then it discusses qiyas of the jurists: it is also probable because the common attribute may not be the actual cause, or the cause may be specific to one case. If the cause is known to be general, then it reduces to the first type (inference from universal to particular). Finally, the gloss comments on Al-Razi's statement that qiyas of the jurists is composed of the first two: it infers from a particular to a universal (like istiqra') and then from the universal to another particular, but it is not certain.

استقراء
قياس الفقهاء
تمثيل

Al-Razi classifies inference into three types: logical syllogism, induction, and juristic analogy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about a recent decision you made or an argument you had. Did you rely more on logical deduction (syllogism), pattern recognition (induction), or comparing to a similar case (analogy)? How does identifying that type help you see a possible weakness in your reasoning?
?
This lesson shows that the word qiyas can mean two different things in Islamic thought. Have you ever used a term loosely without realizing it had a more precise meaning? How can you commit to being more precise with the terms you use in discussions about faith or knowledge?

Induction: Certainty vs Probability
Explanation of complete and incomplete induction and their epistemic status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between complete and incomplete induction and why the former yields certainty while the latter only probability.

Core Idea
Induction yields certainty only when all particulars are examined; otherwise it is merely probable.

The gloss delves into the nature of induction, distinguishing two kinds based on exhaustiveness. Through the famous example of animal jaw movement, we see how incomplete induction can fail. This lesson clarifies the epistemic status of inductive reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on induction
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the definition and the two types of induction.
The passage defines istiqra' and then splits it into complete and incomplete.
2
Understand the example
Examine the example of animals moving their lower jaw. Why does it show probability? What counterexample is given?
The crocodile moves its upper jaw, showing the generalization fails.
3
Compare the two types
Contrast complete induction (e.g., even/odd numbers) with incomplete induction. Note that complete induction is certain, incomplete is probable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : يريد إيراد جمع الحجج مفصلة بقول موجز وهو في الغاية الحسن والبلاغة ، وذلك أنه قسم الاستدلالات إلى ثلاثة أقسام قياس المنطقيين والاستقراء وقياس الفقهاء المسمى عند المنطقيين بالتمثيل وأخر القول في القسم الأول، أما الاستقراء فهو الحكم على كل ما ثبت جزئياته ، فإن كانت الجزئيات محصورة سمي بالاستقراء التام والقياس المقسم كقولنا العدد إما زوج وإما فرد ، وكل زوج يعد بالواحد وكل فرد يعد بالواحد وكل عدد يعد بالواحد وهذا يقيني ، وإن لم تكن الجزئيات محصورة فذلك الحكم يكون ظنيا لاحتمال أن يكون جزئي غير ما ذكر بخلاف ما ذكر والمثال المشهور فيه الحكم بأن كل حيوان يحرك فكه الأسفل عند المضغ لكون الناس وجميع البهائم والسباع كذلك وذلك الحكم غير يقيني ربما يقع فيه مخالفة في جزئي غير هذه الجزئيات كالتمساح ، فإنه يحرك الفك الأعلى عند المضغ وأما قياس الفقهاء فظني أيضا لأن ثبوت الحكم في إحدى الصورتين لا يدل على أن علة ذلك الثبوت هو الأمر المشترك ، ولو ثبت أن المشترك علة لذلك الثبوت فمن الجائز أن يكون علة خاصة بتلك الصورة ، أعني يكون خصوصية تلك الصورة شرطا في عليتها أما إن ثبت أن عليته للحكم عام حيث كان هذا القسم إلى القسم الأول أعني الاستدلال بالكلي على جزئياته ، وصار ذكر الصورة بكون الحكم فيه لها ثابتا حشوا لا تأثير له أصلا، وإنما يختص هذا بالانتهاء لأنهم يكتفون بحصول الظن ولا يستعمله جميعهم أيضا . رجع . أما قوله : « هو بالحقيقة مركب من الأولين ، فلأنه يستدل فيه من جزئي على كلي كاف الاستقراء إلا أن الاستقراء لا يقتضى على جزئي واحد ثم يستدل من ذلك الكلي على الجزئي الآخر ، وذلك أيضا ليس يقينيا فهي مركب ما يشبه الأولين وليس مهما .
I say: He intends to present a collection of proofs in detail with a concise statement, and it is in the utmost beauty and eloquence. That is, he divided inferences into three types: qiyas of the logicians, istiqra', and qiyas of the jurists, called tamthil by the logicians. He deferred the discussion of the first type. As for istiqra', it is judging about everything whose particulars are established. If the particulars are limited, it is called complete induction and the syllogism of division, as when we say: number is either even or odd, and every even is counted by one, and every odd is counted by one, and every number is counted by one — this is certain. But if the particulars are not limited, then that judgment is probable, due to the possibility that there is a particular other than what was mentioned, contrary to what was mentioned. The famous example is the judgment that every animal moves its lower jaw when chewing, because humans and all beasts and predators do so. That judgment is not certain; there may occur a discrepancy in a particular other than these particulars, like the crocodile, for it moves its upper jaw when chewing. As for qiyas of the jurists, it is also probable because the establishment of the judgment in one of the two cases does not indicate that the cause of that establishment is the common attribute; and even if it is established that the common attribute is the cause of that establishment, it is possible that it is a cause specific to that case, i.e., the specificity of that case is a condition for its causality. But if it is established that its causality for the judgment is general, then this type falls under the first type, i.e., inference from the universal to its particulars, and the mention of the case as having the judgment becomes redundant, having no effect at all. This is only specific to the terminological usage because they are content with obtaining probability, and not all of them use it either. Return. As for his statement: 'It is in reality composed of the first two,' it is because in it one infers from a particular to a universal, like the gist of istiqra', except that istiqra' does not require a single particular, then one infers from that universal to the other particular, and that also is not certain. So it is a composite resembling the first two, but it is not them.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This is a marginal gloss explaining Al-Razi's concise statement. It begins by noting that Al-Razi divides inferences into three types: (1) qiyas of the logicians, (2) istiqra' (induction), and (3) qiyas of the jurists (called tamthil by logicians). The gloss then elaborates on istiqra': if the enumeration of particulars is exhaustive (complete induction), it yields certainty, as in the division of numbers into even and odd. If not exhaustive, it yields probability, as in the generalization about animals moving their lower jaw when chewing, which is refuted by the crocodile moving its upper jaw. Then it discusses qiyas of the jurists: it is also probable because the common attribute may not be the actual cause, or the cause may be specific to one case. If the cause is known to be general, then it reduces to the first type (inference from universal to particular). Finally, the gloss comments on Al-Razi's statement that qiyas of the jurists is composed of the first two: it infers from a particular to a universal (like istiqra') and then from the universal to another particular, but it is not certain.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think induction always provides certain knowledge.
Correction: Induction is certain only when all particulars are examined; otherwise it is merely probable, as shown by the crocodile counterexample.

Checkpoint
Why is the generalization about animal jaw movement considered probable rather than certain?
A) Because it is based on a small sample
B) Because a counterexample (crocodile) exists
C) Because it includes all animals
D) Because it is about chewing

Key Takeaway
Complete induction provides certainty; incomplete induction provides only probability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often make judgments based on limited experience—about people, situations, even your own abilities. Can you name one such judgment you hold as certain? What would it mean for you to treat it as merely probable?
?
The crocodile falsified a seemingly universal claim about animals. Is there a belief you have never questioned because it seemed obvious from your experience? Will you commit to actively looking for a 'crocodile' that might challenge it?
?
If complete induction is impossible in most of life, how should that shape the way you speak about your opinions? Are you willing to replace phrases like 'I know that...' with 'It is probable that...' starting today?

Qiyas al-Fuqaha': Probability and Composite Nature
Explanation of juristic analogy and its composite nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why juristic analogy (qiyas al-fuqaha') yields probabilistic knowledge and how it is composed of elements from syllogism and induction.

Core Idea
Juristic analogy is probable, and if the cause is universal it collapses into syllogism.

The gloss continues to examine the third type of inference: qiyas al-fuqaha' (analogy used by jurists). It explains why this method yields only probable conclusions and discusses its relation to the other two types. Finally, it comments on al-Razi's statement that it is composed of the first two types.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on qiyas al-fuqaha'
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the reasoning for why it is probable.
The key point is that the common attribute may not be the actual cause.
2
Understand the probabilistic nature
The gloss gives two reasons: the common attribute may not be the cause, or the cause may be specific to the original case.
3
Examine the composite nature
Read the final part where al-Razi's statement is explained: it moves from particular to universal (like induction) then from universal to particular (like syllogism).
This shows why it is not certain.

The Two Main Divisions of Qiyas: Conditional and Conjunctive
Al-Razi divides qiyas into conditional and conjunctive syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two fundamental types of qiyas—conditional (istithna'i) and conjunctive (iqtirani)—as defined by Imam al-Razi.

Core Idea
Qiyas is essentially either conditional or conjunctive.

Imam al-Razi begins his discussion of qiyas by dividing it into two primary categories. This classification is the cornerstone of Islamic logic, determining how premises combine to yield conclusions. The passage defines each type with clarity, setting the stage for further subdivisions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get the rhythm. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the definitions of 'istithna'i' and 'iqtirani'.
The passage is short but packed with technical terms.
2
Identify the two definitions
Find where al-Razi defines the conditional syllogism and the conjunctive syllogism. Note how the conditional includes an exception (istithna') while the conjunctive does not.
The conditional has one premise that is conditional and the other that mentions the exception.
3
Contrast the two types
Reflect on the key difference: the conditional involves an 'if...then' structure with an exceptive clause, while the conjunctive simply combines two categorical premises.
This fundamental distinction drives all subsequent divisions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم القياس بالمعنى الأول ينقسم إلى استثنائي واقتراني فالاستثنائي ما تركب من مقدمتين إحداهما شرطية والأخرى مقرونة بالاستثناء وتكون عين أحد طرفي الشرطية أو نقيضها ، والاقتراني هو الذي لا يكون كذلك ، والاستثنائي ينقسم إلى متصلة ومنفصلة ، وفي المتصلة يحتمل أن يكون التالي وهو اللازم أعم من المقدم الذي هو الملزوم كوجود العلم ووجود الحياة فيستدل من غير الملزوم ومن غير اللازم ولا يستدل من وجود اللازم ولا من عدم الملزوم . وقد أورد ذكر المنتج منها وغير المنتج في كلمات قليلة، وفي المنفصلة يستدل بعين كل واحد على نقيض الآخر وبالعكس فينتج أربع نتائج . وأما الاقتراني فلابد في مقدمتيه من جزء مشترك بينهما ومن جزء خاص لكل واحدة منهما فيتناسقا وإذا التقى المشترك ثبت الحكم المطلوب بين المنافيتين وهو النتيجة وينقسم إلى أربعة أقسام محكوما به في إحداهما محكوما عليه في الأخرى وإما محكوما به فيهما وإما محكوما عليه فيهما ، والأول ينقسم إلى ما يكون المشترك محكوما به في المقدمة التي يكون التالي من جزئها محكوما عليه في النتيجة ويسمى شكلا أولا ، وإما أن يكون بعكس ذلك ويسمى شكلا رابعا، ولا يورد في أكثر الكتب لبعده عن الطبع، وأما إذا كان المشترك محكوما به فيهما فيسمى شكلا ثانيا ، وإذا كان محكوما عليه فيهما يسمى شكلا ثالثا والعمدة هو الأول وينتج منه أربعة ضروب، لأن المقدمة التي تشتمل على المحكوم عليه في النتيجة يجب أن تكون موجبة كلية أو جزئية ، والأخرى يجب أن تكون كلية موجبة أو سالبة ، والنتائج أربع موجبة كلية وموجبة جزئية وسالبة كلية وسالبة جزئية، وقد أورد ذلك في ألفاظ قليلة في غاية الإيجاز، والثاني أورده على سبيل الاختصار ، والمنتجة
Then qiyas in the first sense divides into istithna'i (conditional) and iqtirani (conjunctive). The istithna'i is that which is composed of two propositions, one of which is conditional and the other is connected with the [؟] exception? and is the very one of the two parts of the conditional or its contradictory. The iqtirani is that which is not like that. The istithna'i divides into connected (mutasila) and disjunctive (munfasila). In the connected, it is possible that the consequent, which is the necessary, is more general than the antecedent, which is the necessitated, like the existence of knowledge and existence of life; so one infers from the non-existence of the necessitated and non-existence of the necessary, but one does not infer from the existence of the necessary nor from the non-existence of the necessitated. He presented the mention of the productive and non-productive among them in few words. In the disjunctive, one infers from the very each one to the contradictory of the other and vice versa, producing four conclusions. As for the iqtirani, it is necessary in its two premises to have a common part between them and a special part for each one of them, so they are co-extensive? And when the common part meets, the desired judgment between the two opposing things is established, which is the conclusion. It divides into four types: the common part is judged as predicate in one of them and as subject in the other, or as predicate in both, or as subject in both. The first divides into the case where the common is predicate in the premise whose consequent from its part is predicate in the conclusion, and it is called the first figure; or it is the reverse of that, and it is called the fourth figure, and it is not mentioned in most books due to its unnaturalness. And if the common is predicate in both, it is called the second figure; and if it is subject in both, it is called the third figure. The primary is the first, and it produces four modes, because the premise that contains the subject in the conclusion must be universal affirmative or particular, and the other must be universal affirmative or negative. The conclusions are four: universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, particular negative. He presented that in very concise words. The second (figure) he presented in a summarized manner. The productive
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage deals with the subdivision of qiyas (syllogism) in the first sense (i.e., syllogism proper). Al-Razi divides it into istithna'i (conditional syllogism) and iqtirani (conjunctive syllogism). The conditional syllogism is composed of two premises, one of which is conditional, and the other is connected with the exception? and is either the same as one part of the conditional or its opposite. The conditional syllogism is further divided into connected (mutasila) and disjunctive (munfasila). In the connected conditional syllogism, the consequent may be more general than the antecedent (as knowledge and life), so one can infer from the non-existence of the antecedent or the non-existence of the consequent? Actually he says one infers from the non-existence of the antecedent (al-malzum) and from the non-existence of the necessary (al-lazim)? Wait, read carefully: 'fi al-muttasila yuhtamal an yakun al-tali huwa al-lazim a?amm min al-muqaddam... fa-yustadall min ghayr al-malzum wa min ghayr al-lazim wa la yustadall min wujud al-lazim wa la min 'adam al-malzum' – This is slightly ambiguous. It means: in the connected conditional, the consequent (the necessary) may be more general than the antecedent (the necessitated); one can infer from the non-existence of the necessitated? Actually 'min ghayr al-malzum' could mean 'from the non-existence of the necessitated' or 'from something other than the necessitated'? Typically, the standard rules are: from existence of antecedent, existence of consequent; from non-existence of consequent, non-existence of antecedent. But the text says: 'yustadall min ghayr al-malzum wa min ghayr al-lazim' – this is odd. Perhaps it means one can infer from the non-existence of the antecedent and from the non-existence of the consequent? Or perhaps it's a corruption. The commentator clarifies that he mentioned the productive and non-productive in few words. Then for the disjunctive, each part implies the negation of the other, so four conclusions are possible. Then Al-Razi moves to the conjunctive syllogism (iqtirani), which requires a common term in both premises. When the common term 'meets' (i.e., is identified), the conclusion is established. The conjunctive syllogism is divided into four figures based on whether the common term is predicate or subject in each premise. The first figure has the common term as predicate in the major premise and subject in the minor? Actually the description: 'al-mushtarak mahkuman bihi fi al-muqaddimah allati yakun al-tali min juz'iha mahkuman 'alayhi fi al-natijah' – this needs careful parsing. He says the first figure is when the common term is predicate in the premise whose consequent from its part is subject in the conclusion? This is technical. Essentially, he outlines the traditional four figures: first (natural), second (common predicate), third (common subject), fourth (reverse of first, unnatural). The first figure is primary and yields four valid moods. He ends by saying he has summarized this very concisely. The text cuts off at 'al-muntijah' (the productive).

Key Terms
قياس استثنائي
قياس اقتراني

Checkpoint

Subdivisions of Conditional Syllogisms: Connected and Disjunctive
Al-Razi divides conditional syllogisms into connected and disjunctive with inference rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two subtypes of conditional syllogisms—connected (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila)—and the valid patterns of inference for each.

Core Idea
Conditional syllogisms are either connected or disjunctive, each with distinct inference rules.

Having defined conditional syllogisms, al-Razi now elaborates their internal structure. A conditional syllogism can be either connected (if-then) or disjunctive (either-or). Each type has specific rules for deriving valid conclusions, which he illustrates with examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text and translation. Focus on the two types: muttasila and munfasila.
The passage includes a remark about the possibility of the consequent being more general than the antecedent.
2
Understand inference in the connected type
Note the rule: from the non-existence of the antecedent (malzum) or the non-existence of the consequent (lazim) one can infer. But the text says one cannot infer from the existence of the consequent or the non-existence of the antecedent—this is a special case when the consequent is more general.
This might seem counterintuitive; it reflects a specific logical nuance.
3
Understand inference in the disjunctive type
In the disjunctive, each part implies the contradiction of the other, and vice versa, yielding four possible conclusions.
For example, from 'either A or B' and 'A', we infer 'not B', etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم القياس بالمعنى الأول ينقسم إلى استثنائي واقتراني فالاستثنائي ما تركب من مقدمتين إحداهما شرطية والأخرى مقرونة بالاستثناء وتكون عين أحد طرفي الشرطية أو نقيضها ، والاقتراني هو الذي لا يكون كذلك ، والاستثنائي ينقسم إلى متصلة ومنفصلة ، وفي المتصلة يحتمل أن يكون التالي وهو اللازم أعم من المقدم الذي هو الملزوم كوجود العلم ووجود الحياة فيستدل من غير الملزوم ومن غير اللازم ولا يستدل من وجود اللازم ولا من عدم الملزوم . وقد أورد ذكر المنتج منها وغير المنتج في كلمات قليلة، وفي المنفصلة يستدل بعين كل واحد على نقيض الآخر وبالعكس فينتج أربع نتائج . وأما الاقتراني فلابد في مقدمتيه من جزء مشترك بينهما ومن جزء خاص لكل واحدة منهما فيتناسقا وإذا التقى المشترك ثبت الحكم المطلوب بين المنافيتين وهو النتيجة وينقسم إلى أربعة أقسام محكوما به في إحداهما محكوما عليه في الأخرى وإما محكوما به فيهما وإما محكوما عليه فيهما ، والأول ينقسم إلى ما يكون المشترك محكوما به في المقدمة التي يكون التالي من جزئها محكوما عليه في النتيجة ويسمى شكلا أولا ، وإما أن يكون بعكس ذلك ويسمى شكلا رابعا، ولا يورد في أكثر الكتب لبعده عن الطبع، وأما إذا كان المشترك محكوما به فيهما فيسمى شكلا ثانيا ، وإذا كان محكوما عليه فيهما يسمى شكلا ثالثا والعمدة هو الأول وينتج منه أربعة ضروب، لأن المقدمة التي تشتمل على المحكوم عليه في النتيجة يجب أن تكون موجبة كلية أو جزئية ، والأخرى يجب أن تكون كلية موجبة أو سالبة ، والنتائج أربع موجبة كلية وموجبة جزئية وسالبة كلية وسالبة جزئية، وقد أورد ذلك في ألفاظ قليلة في غاية الإيجاز، والثاني أورده على سبيل الاختصار ، والمنتجة
Then qiyas in the first sense divides into istithna'i (conditional) and iqtirani (conjunctive). The istithna'i is that which is composed of two propositions, one of which is conditional and the other is connected with the [؟] exception? and is the very one of the two parts of the conditional or its contradictory. The iqtirani is that which is not like that. The istithna'i divides into connected (mutasila) and disjunctive (munfasila). In the connected, it is possible that the consequent, which is the necessary, is more general than the antecedent, which is the necessitated, like the existence of knowledge and existence of life; so one infers from the non-existence of the necessitated and non-existence of the necessary, but one does not infer from the existence of the necessary nor from the non-existence of the necessitated. He presented the mention of the productive and non-productive among them in few words. In the disjunctive, one infers from the very each one to the contradictory of the other and vice versa, producing four conclusions. As for the iqtirani, it is necessary in its two premises to have a common part between them and a special part for each one of them, so they are co-extensive? And when the common part meets, the desired judgment between the two opposing things is established, which is the conclusion. It divides into four types: the common part is judged as predicate in one of them and as subject in the other, or as predicate in both, or as subject in both. The first divides into the case where the common is predicate in the premise whose consequent from its part is predicate in the conclusion, and it is called the first figure; or it is the reverse of that, and it is called the fourth figure, and it is not mentioned in most books due to its unnaturalness. And if the common is predicate in both, it is called the second figure; and if it is subject in both, it is called the third figure. The primary is the first, and it produces four modes, because the premise that contains the subject in the conclusion must be universal affirmative or particular, and the other must be universal affirmative or negative. The conclusions are four: universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, particular negative. He presented that in very concise words. The second (figure) he presented in a summarized manner. The productive
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage deals with the subdivision of qiyas (syllogism) in the first sense (i.e., syllogism proper). Al-Razi divides it into istithna'i (conditional syllogism) and iqtirani (conjunctive syllogism). The conditional syllogism is composed of two premises, one of which is conditional, and the other is connected with the exception? and is either the same as one part of the conditional or its opposite. The conditional syllogism is further divided into connected (mutasila) and disjunctive (munfasila). In the connected conditional syllogism, the consequent may be more general than the antecedent (as knowledge and life), so one can infer from the non-existence of the antecedent or the non-existence of the consequent? Actually he says one infers from the non-existence of the antecedent (al-malzum) and from the non-existence of the necessary (al-lazim)? Wait, read carefully: 'fi al-muttasila yuhtamal an yakun al-tali huwa al-lazim a?amm min al-muqaddam... fa-yustadall min ghayr al-malzum wa min ghayr al-lazim wa la yustadall min wujud al-lazim wa la min 'adam al-malzum' – This is slightly ambiguous. It means: in the connected conditional, the consequent (the necessary) may be more general than the antecedent (the necessitated); one can infer from the non-existence of the necessitated? Actually 'min ghayr al-malzum' could mean 'from the non-existence of the necessitated' or 'from something other than the necessitated'? Typically, the standard rules are: from existence of antecedent, existence of consequent; from non-existence of consequent, non-existence of antecedent. But the text says: 'yustadall min ghayr al-malzum wa min ghayr al-lazim' – this is odd. Perhaps it means one can infer from the non-existence of the antecedent and from the non-existence of the consequent? Or perhaps it's a corruption. The commentator clarifies that he mentioned the productive and non-productive in few words. Then for the disjunctive, each part implies the negation of the other, so four conclusions are possible. Then Al-Razi moves to the conjunctive syllogism (iqtirani), which requires a common term in both premises. When the common term 'meets' (i.e., is identified), the conclusion is established. The conjunctive syllogism is divided into four figures based on whether the common term is predicate or subject in each premise. The first figure has the common term as predicate in the major premise and subject in the minor? Actually the description: 'al-mushtarak mahkuman bihi fi al-muqaddimah allati yakun al-tali min juz'iha mahkuman 'alayhi fi al-natijah' – this needs careful parsing. He says the first figure is when the common term is predicate in the premise whose consequent from its part is subject in the conclusion? This is technical. Essentially, he outlines the traditional four figures: first (natural), second (common predicate), third (common subject), fourth (reverse of first, unnatural). The first figure is primary and yields four valid moods. He ends by saying he has summarized this very concisely. The text cuts off at 'al-muntijah' (the productive).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: In a connected conditional, one can always infer from the existence of the antecedent to the existence of the consequent.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that when the consequent is more general than the antecedent, this inference is not valid; instead, one infers from non-existence.

A) Two
B) Four
C) Six
D) Eight

Key Takeaway
Conditional syllogisms come in connected and disjunctive forms, each with specific valid inference patterns.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you present an argument for a core belief you hold, do you ever assume that because a necessary condition is absent, the belief itself must be false? Recall a specific instance from your conversations or internal reasoning where you may have fallen into the fallacy of denying the antecedent incorrectly. Commit to checking your inferences using al-Razi's rules.
?
The disjunctive syllogism shows that if one option is true, the other must be false. In matters of faith or practice, do you hold positions that you treat as if they are the only possibilities, without considering that there might be a third option? Reflect on one such binary you accept and ask yourself whether it is truly an exclusive disjunction as required by logic.
?
Al-Razi's example of knowledge and life teaches that a necessary condition can be more general than what it necessitates. In your spiritual life, are there practices or states you treat as identical? For instance, do you assume that outward knowledge always accompanies inner life? Examine one area where you conflate a more general condition with a specific result, and resolve to clarify the distinction in your own heart.

Logic and Technical Terms

Conjunctive Syllogisms: Common Term and Four Figures
Al-Razi explains the conjunctive syllogism, its common term, the four figures, and the first figure's moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how conjunctive syllogisms require a common term and how they are classified into four figures, with special focus on the first figure and its four valid moods.

Core Idea
Conjunctive syllogisms rely on a common term and are arranged into four figures based on its position.

After discussing conditional syllogisms, al-Razi turns to conjunctive syllogisms, which form the core of categorical logic. He explains that every conjunctive syllogism must have a common term shared between the two premises, and the way this term is placed determines the figure. The first figure is the most natural and productive, yielding four valid mood forms.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text and translation carefully. Note the requirement of a common part (juz' mushtarak) between the two premises.
The text describes four divisions based on whether the common term is predicate or subject in each premise.
2
Identify the four figures
Find where al-Razi enumerates the four figures: first (common term predicate in one premise and subject in the other, with the common term being predicate in the premise whose consequent is subject in conclusion), second (common term predicate in both), third (subject in both), fourth (reverse of first, unnatural).
The first figure is emphasized as primary; the fourth is rarely mentioned.
3
Understand the first figure's moods
The first figure yields four moods because the premise containing the subject of the conclusion must be universal affirmative or particular, and the other premise must be universal (affirmative or negative). This produces four combinations.
The four conclusions are universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, particular negative.
4
Note the conciseness
Al-Razi mentions that he presented this very concisely, and the second figure and others are summarized or omitted.
The text ends abruptly at 'al-muntijah' (the productive).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم القياس بالمعنى الأول ينقسم إلى استثنائي واقتراني فالاستثنائي ما تركب من مقدمتين إحداهما شرطية والأخرى مقرونة بالاستثناء وتكون عين أحد طرفي الشرطية أو نقيضها ، والاقتراني هو الذي لا يكون كذلك ، والاستثنائي ينقسم إلى متصلة ومنفصلة ، وفي المتصلة يحتمل أن يكون التالي وهو اللازم أعم من المقدم الذي هو الملزوم كوجود العلم ووجود الحياة فيستدل من غير الملزوم ومن غير اللازم ولا يستدل من وجود اللازم ولا من عدم الملزوم . وقد أورد ذكر المنتج منها وغير المنتج في كلمات قليلة، وفي المنفصلة يستدل بعين كل واحد على نقيض الآخر وبالعكس فينتج أربع نتائج . وأما الاقتراني فلابد في مقدمتيه من جزء مشترك بينهما ومن جزء خاص لكل واحدة منهما فيتناسقا وإذا التقى المشترك ثبت الحكم المطلوب بين المنافيتين وهو النتيجة وينقسم إلى أربعة أقسام محكوما به في إحداهما محكوما عليه في الأخرى وإما محكوما به فيهما وإما محكوما عليه فيهما ، والأول ينقسم إلى ما يكون المشترك محكوما به في المقدمة التي يكون التالي من جزئها محكوما عليه في النتيجة ويسمى شكلا أولا ، وإما أن يكون بعكس ذلك ويسمى شكلا رابعا، ولا يورد في أكثر الكتب لبعده عن الطبع، وأما إذا كان المشترك محكوما به فيهما فيسمى شكلا ثانيا ، وإذا كان محكوما عليه فيهما يسمى شكلا ثالثا والعمدة هو الأول وينتج منه أربعة ضروب، لأن المقدمة التي تشتمل على المحكوم عليه في النتيجة يجب أن تكون موجبة كلية أو جزئية ، والأخرى يجب أن تكون كلية موجبة أو سالبة ، والنتائج أربع موجبة كلية وموجبة جزئية وسالبة كلية وسالبة جزئية، وقد أورد ذلك في ألفاظ قليلة في غاية الإيجاز، والثاني أورده على سبيل الاختصار ، والمنتجة

Complete Induction and Certainty
Definition of complete induction with example and certainty condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that complete induction, when all particulars are enumerated, yields certain knowledge.

Core Idea
Exhaustive enumeration of particulars guarantees certainty.

Before exploring the limitations of induction, we must first understand when it yields absolute certainty. The author presents a simple mathematical example showing that if all cases are accounted for, the conclusion is beyond doubt. This foundational distinction sets the stage for evaluating weaker forms of reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on complete induction
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the example of even and odd numbers.
The example is straightforward but illustrates the principle clearly.
2
Identify the conditions for certainty
The author states that certainty requires the particulars to be exhaustive. Reflect on why this is necessary.
3
Contrast with incomplete induction
The passage immediately contrasts complete induction with incomplete induction. Note the shift from certain to speculative.
This comparison deepens your understanding of the certainty condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاستقراء التام والقياس المقسم كقولنا العدد إما زوج وإما فرد، وكل زوج يعد بالواحد وكل فرد يعد بالواحد وكل عدد يعد بالواحد وهذا يقيني، وإن لم تكن الجزئيات محصورة فذلك الحكم يكون ظنيا لاحتمال أن يكون جزئي غير ما ذكر بخلاف ما ذكر. والمثال المشهور فيه الحكم بأن كل حيوان يحرك فكه الأسفل عند المضغ لكون الناس وجميع البهائم والسباع كذلك وذلك الحكم غير يقيني ربما يقع فيه مخالفة في جزئي غير هذه الجزئيات كالتمساح، فإنه يحرك الفك الأعلى عند المضغ. وأما قياس الفقهاء فظني أيضاً لأن ثبوت الحكم في إحدى الصورتين لا يدل على أن علة ذلك الثبوت هو الأمر المشترك، ولو ثبت أن المشترك علة لذلك الثبوت فمن الجائز أن يكون علة خاصة بتلك الصورة، أعني يكون خصوصية تلك الصورة شرطا في عليتها أما إن ثبت أن عليته للحكم عام حيث كان هذا القسم إلى القسم الأول أعني الاستدلال بالكلي على جزئياته، وصار ذكر الصورة بكون الحكم فيه لها ثابتا حشوا لا تأثير له أصلا، وإنما يختص هذا بالانتهاء لأنهم يكتفون بحصول الظن ولا يستعمله جميعهم أيضاً. رجع أما قوله: «هو بالحقيقة مركب من الأولين»، فلأنه يستدل فيه بمجزئي على كلي كافي الاستقراء إلا أن الاستقراء لا يقتضي على جزئي واحد ثم يستدل من ذلك الكلي على الجزئي الآخر، وذلك أيضاً ليس يقينيا فهي مركب ما يشبه الأولين وليس مهما.
Complete induction and the divided syllogism, like our saying: the number is either even or odd, and every even is counted by one, and every odd is counted by one, and every number is counted by one—this is certain. But if the particulars are not exhaustive, then that judgment is speculative due to the possibility that there is a particular other than what was mentioned, contrary to what was mentioned. The famous example of it is the judgment that every animal moves its lower jaw when chewing, because humans and all beasts and predators are so; this judgment is not certain, perhaps a violation occurs in a particular other than these particulars, like the crocodile, for it moves the upper jaw when chewing. As for the analogy of the jurists, it is also speculative because the establishment of a judgment in one of the two cases does not indicate that the cause of that establishment is the common factor; and even if it is established that the common factor is the cause of that establishment, it is possible that the cause is specific to that case, meaning that the particularity of that case is a condition for its causality. But if it is established that its causality for the judgment is general, since this section belongs to the first section, namely inference from the universal to its particulars, then the mention of the case with the judgment being established in it becomes redundant, having no effect at all. This is restricted to the end, because they are satisfied with obtaining speculation, and not all of them use it. He returned: As for his saying: 'It is in reality composed of the first two', because in it one infers from a particular to a universal sufficient for induction, except that induction does not necessitate a single particular, then one infers from that universal to the other particular. That is also not certain, so it is a composite resembling the first two and is not important.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage defines and explains types of induction and analogical reasoning. The author first describes complete induction (al-istiqra' al-tamm) and the divided syllogism (al-qiyas al-muqassam), giving the example of numbers to show that when the enumeration is exhaustive, the conclusion is certain (yaqini). Then he notes that if the particulars are not exhaustive, the judgment is speculative (dhanni) because a counterexample may exist. The famous example is the movement of the lower jaw during chewing: observing humans and most animals leads to the general claim, but the crocodile is an exception, moving its upper jaw. Next, he discusses the analogy of jurists (qiyas al-fuqaha'), which is also speculative because even if a common attribute is identified as the cause (illa), it might be specific to the original case, and establishing generality requires the inference to be from the universal to the particulars, making the specific case redundant. Finally, he addresses a quoted objection that this method is composed of the first two (induction and deduction), explaining that it infers from a particular to a universal (incomplete induction) and then from that universal to another particular, which is not certain and thus unimportant.

Key Takeaway
Complete induction, which covers all particulars, yields certainty.

Incomplete Induction and Its Limitations
Explanation of incomplete induction as speculative, with crocodile example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that when particulars are not exhaustively enumerated, induction yields only speculative knowledge, and you will recognize the crocodile counterexample.

Core Idea
Incomplete induction is probable, not certain—a single counterexample can refute it.

Most inductive generalizations in daily life are based on limited observations. The author now explains why such reasoning remains uncertain, using a memorable example: the claim that all animals move their lower jaw when chewing is falsified by the crocodile. This lesson clarifies the crucial distinction between certainty and probability in induction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on incomplete induction
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the condition that makes the judgment speculative.
The crocodile example is famous and worth remembering.
2
Understand the probabilistic nature
The author says the judgment is 'dhanni' (speculative) because a counterexample may exist. Think about why this is not certain.
3
Analyze the crocodile counterexample
The crocodile moves its upper jaw, disproving the generalization. Note that the exception occurs in a particular not included in the initial observation.
This illustrates the weakness of incomplete induction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاستقراء التام والقياس المقسم كقولنا العدد إما زوج وإما فرد، وكل زوج يعد بالواحد وكل فرد يعد بالواحد وكل عدد يعد بالواحد وهذا يقيني، وإن لم تكن الجزئيات محصورة فذلك الحكم يكون ظنيا لاحتمال أن يكون جزئي غير ما ذكر بخلاف ما ذكر. والمثال المشهور فيه الحكم بأن كل حيوان يحرك فكه الأسفل عند المضغ لكون الناس وجميع البهائم والسباع كذلك وذلك الحكم غير يقيني ربما يقع فيه مخالفة في جزئي غير هذه الجزئيات كالتمساح، فإنه يحرك الفك الأعلى عند المضغ. وأما قياس الفقهاء فظني أيضاً لأن ثبوت الحكم في إحدى الصورتين لا يدل على أن علة ذلك الثبوت هو الأمر المشترك، ولو ثبت أن المشترك علة لذلك الثبوت فمن الجائز أن يكون علة خاصة بتلك الصورة، أعني يكون خصوصية تلك الصورة شرطا في عليتها أما إن ثبت أن عليته للحكم عام حيث كان هذا القسم إلى القسم الأول أعني الاستدلال بالكلي على جزئياته، وصار ذكر الصورة بكون الحكم فيه لها ثابتا حشوا لا تأثير له أصلا، وإنما يختص هذا بالانتهاء لأنهم يكتفون بحصول الظن ولا يستعمله جميعهم أيضاً. رجع أما قوله: «هو بالحقيقة مركب من الأولين»، فلأنه يستدل فيه بمجزئي على كلي كافي الاستقراء إلا أن الاستقراء لا يقتضي على جزئي واحد ثم يستدل من ذلك الكلي على الجزئي الآخر، وذلك أيضاً ليس يقينيا فهي مركب ما يشبه الأولين وليس مهما.
Complete induction and the divided syllogism, like our saying: the number is either even or odd, and every even is counted by one, and every odd is counted by one, and every number is counted by one—this is certain. But if the particulars are not exhaustive, then that judgment is speculative due to the possibility that there is a particular other than what was mentioned, contrary to what was mentioned. The famous example of it is the judgment that every animal moves its lower jaw when chewing, because humans and all beasts and predators are so; this judgment is not certain, perhaps a violation occurs in a particular other than these particulars, like the crocodile, for it moves the upper jaw when chewing. As for the analogy of the jurists, it is also speculative because the establishment of a judgment in one of the two cases does not indicate that the cause of that establishment is the common factor; and even if it is established that the common factor is the cause of that establishment, it is possible that the cause is specific to that case, meaning that the particularity of that case is a condition for its causality. But if it is established that its causality for the judgment is general, since this section belongs to the first section, namely inference from the universal to its particulars, then the mention of the case with the judgment being established in it becomes redundant, having no effect at all. This is restricted to the end, because they are satisfied with obtaining speculation, and not all of them use it. He returned: As for his saying: 'It is in reality composed of the first two', because in it one infers from a particular to a universal sufficient for induction, except that induction does not necessitate a single particular, then one infers from that universal to the other particular. Commentary
This passage defines and explains types of induction and analogical reasoning. The author first describes complete induction (al-istiqra' al-tamm) and the divided syllogism (al-qiyas al-muqassam), giving the example of numbers to show that when the enumeration is exhaustive, the conclusion is certain (yaqini). Analogical Reasoning of Jurists
Analysis of juristic analogy as speculative and its logical structure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why analogical reasoning in jurisprudence (qiyas al-fuqaha') is speculative and why it differs from certain inference.

Core Idea
Analogy in law is speculative because the common cause may not be generalizable.

The third method discussed is the analogy used by Islamic jurists. This type of reasoning extends a ruling from one case to another based on a shared attribute. The author explains why such reasoning remains probable rather than certain: even if the common attribute is the cause, it might be limited to the original case. This lesson unpacks the logical structure of juristic analogy and its limitations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on juristic analogy
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that the author says it is speculative (dhanni).
The passage assumes some familiarity with legal reasoning.
2
Understand the two potential problems
First, the common attribute may not be the cause. Second, even if it is, the cause might be specific to the original case.
These are two separate objections.
3
Consider the redundancy point
If the common attribute's causality is established generally, the original case becomes redundant—this shows the method reduces to universal inference.
This is a subtle logical point.
4
Reflect on the composite nature
The author references a view that juristic analogy is composed of induction and deduction, but finds it unimportant.
This final step connects to the earlier types.

Source Text
